Imamate: Not Fundamental but Obligatory
Al-Shahrastani states that imamate is not a fundamental belief yet warns against error in it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Al-Shahrastani considers imamate not a fundamental principle of belief, yet still emphasizes its obligatory nature and the severe danger of error in it.

Core Idea
Imamate is not a pillar of faith, but error in it is more dangerous than ignorance.

Al-Shahrastani opens his discussion on imamate by clarifying its theological status. He distinguishes between core creedal matters and practical obligations, warning that while imamate is not a fundamental principle of belief, mistakes about it carry greater risk than ignorance. This passage sets the stage for his broader argument by citing the groups who affirm its obligation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its literal translation. Notice the careful distinction Al-Shahrastani makes between imamate and fundamental beliefs.
Pay attention to the phrase 'ليست من أصول الاعتقاد' – this is the key claim.
2
Identify the two-part structure
The passage has two main parts: (1) a statement on imamate's status and (2) a list of groups who affirm its obligation. See how the warning about danger connects them.
3
Understand the danger warning
Al-Shahrastani says error in imamate is more dangerous than ignorance of its principle. Reflect on why: because it involves specific, contested claims vulnerable to partisan bias.
The phrase 'التعسف الصادر عن الأهواء المضلة' points to the cause.
4
Review the cited groups
Note the diverse groups: Ash'ari hadith scholars, jurists, some Shi'a, Mu'tazila, and most Khawarij. This consensus shows that despite not being a fundamental belief, imamate is widely held as obligatory.
The passage is cut off after mentioning 'Ahl al-Sunna', but the point is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القول في الإمامة : اعلم أن الإمامة ليست من أصول الاعتقاد ؛ بحيث يفضي النظر فيها إلى قطع ويقين بالتعين، ولكن الخطر على من يخطي فيها يزيد على الخطر على من يجهل أصلها والتعسف الصادر عن الأهواء المضلة مانع من الإنصاف فيها. وقد قال جمهور أصحاب الحديث من الأشعرية والفقهاء وجماعة الشيعة والمعتزلة وأكثر الخوارج بوجوبها فرضا من الله تعالى، ثم جماعة أهل السنة
The discourse on imamate: Know that imamate is not among the foundations of belief, such that speculation about it leads to decisive certainty of designation, but the danger for one who errs in it exceeds the danger for one who is ignorant of its principle, and the arbitrariness arising from misguiding passions prevents fairness in it. And the majority of hadith scholars among the Ash'aris, and the jurists, and a group of the Shi'a, and the Mu'tazila, and most of the Khawarij have said that it is obligatory as a duty from God Most High, then the group of Ahl al-Sunna...
Source pages p. 477

Commentary
This passage defines the status of imamate. It begins with the statement that imamate is not among the fundamental principles of belief (usul al-i'tiqad) such that theoretical investigation leads to certain knowledge of a specific individual. However, the author immediately adds a caution: the danger for one who errs in this matter is greater than the danger for one who is ignorant of its very principle. This is because arbitrariness arising from misguiding passions prevents fairness in this discussion. The author then lists the groups who affirm the obligation (wujub) of imamate as a duty from God: the majority of Ash'ari hadith scholars, jurists, a group of Shi'a, Mu'tazila, and most Khawarij. The text then begins to mention 'then the group of Ahl al-Sunna' but is cut off at the page end. The passage establishes that although imamate is not a core creedal matter like tawhid or prophethood, it is still obligatory and error in it carries serious consequences.

Key Terms

أصول الاعتقاد
وجوب
فرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that since imamate is not a fundamental belief, it is unimportant or optional.
Correction: Al-Shahrastani explicitly warns that the danger of error in imamate is greater than ignorance of its very principle, meaning it carries serious consequences and is not optional.

Checkpoint
According to the author, which groups affirm the obligation of imamate?
A) Only the Ash'aris and Mu'tazila.
B) Majority of Ash'ari hadith scholars, jurists, some Shi'a, Mu'tazila, and most Khawarij.
C) Only Ahl al-Sunna and Shi'a.
D) All Muslims except the Khawarij.

Key Takeaway
Imamate is not among the foundational beliefs of Islam, but it is an obligatory duty from God, and error in it is more dangerous than total ignorance of its principle.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed a religious matter as unimportant simply because it is not a core belief? This passage warns that error in a non-core matter can be more dangerous than ignorance. Is there an area of Islamic teaching you have treated as secondary, yet your wrong stance on it might be harming your faith or community? What will you do about it?
?
Al-Shahrastani says arbitrariness from passions prevents fairness in discussing imamate. Where in your own life have your passions—loyalty to a group, school of thought, or leader—prevented you from being fair or objective about a religious issue? Will you commit to seeking truth even when it goes against your inclinations?
?
The passage lists a broad consensus that imamate is obligatory. Have you ever felt that your personal opinion on a matter of ummatic leadership is acceptable even if it contradicts the consensus of scholars? How does this passage challenge your confidence in your own views, and what step can you take to align yourself with the truth?

The Imamate: Status, Obligation, and Functions
Defines imamate, states its non-creedal status, reports consensus on obligation, and lists functions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of imamate, its status as a non-fundamental belief, the consensus on its obligation, and the comprehensive functions of the imam according to Ahl al-Sunna.

Core Idea
Imamate is obligatory but not a core belief; its necessity is practical and societal.

This passage defines the imamate and clarifies its place in Islamic theology. The author first distinguishes it from core beliefs like tawhid and prophethood, then establishes the scholarly consensus on its obligation. Finally, he enumerates the essential roles of the imam, demonstrating why leadership is indispensable for the Muslim community. Let's explore this foundational teaching on political theology.

Guided Path
1
Understand the status of imamate
Read the opening statement: imamate is not from usul al-i'tiqad. Reflect on why the author emphasizes its non-foundational nature.
This prevents confusion with creedal essentials.
2
Identify the consensus
Locate the sentence mentioning groups who affirm obligation. Notice the wide agreement across sects.
Even Shi'a and Khawarij, despite disagreements, agree on obligation.
3
Note the Sunni position
Read the specific view of Ahl al-Sunna: it is a collective duty to establish an imam and obey him.
The term 'al-mansur' may mean 'the one given victory' or refer to the rightful imam.
4
Examine the list of functions
Carefully go through each function: legal judgments, hudud, protection, armies, etc. Consider why each is necessary.
This list shows the comprehensive scope of imamate authority.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القول في الإمامة: اعلم أن الإمامة ليست من أصول الاعتقاد؛ بحيث يفضي النظر فيها إلى قطع ويقين بالتعين، ولكن الخطر على من يخطي فيها يزيد على الخطر على من يجهل أصلها والتعسف الصادر عن الأهواء المضلة مانع من الإنصاف فيها. وقد قال جمهور أصحاب الحديث من الأشعرية والفقهاء وجماعة الشيعة والمعتزلة وأكثر الخوارج بوجوبها فرضا من الله تعالى، ثم جماعة أهل السنة قالوا هو فرض واجب على المسلمين إقامته واتباع المنصور فرض واجب عليهم؛ إذ لا بد لكافتهم من إمام ينفذ أحكامهم، ويقيم حدودهم، ويحفظ بيضتهم، ويحرس حوزتهم، ويعبي جيوشهم، ويقسم غنائمهم وصدقاتهم، ويتحاكموا إليه في خصوماتهم ومناكحاتهم، ويراعي فيه أمور الجمع والأعياد، وينصف المظلوم وينتصف من الظالم، وينصب القضاة والولاة في كل ناحية، ويبعث القراءة والدعاة إلى كل طرف.
The chapter on the imamate: Know that the imamate is not among the principles of belief, such that investigation into it leads to decisive certainty of [specific] designation. However, the danger for one who errs in it is greater than the danger for one who is ignorant of its foundation, and the partisanship arising from misleading passions prevents fairness in it. And the majority of the Hadith folk from the Ash'aris, the jurists, a group of the Shi'a, the Mu'tazila, and most of the Khawarij have said it is obligatory as a duty from Allah, exalted. Then the group of Ahl al-Sunna said: it is an obligatory duty upon the Muslims to establish it, and following the one who is given victory is an obligatory duty upon them, because there must inevitably be for all of them an imam who executes their judgments, establishes their legal punishments, protects their territory, guards their domain, organizes their armies, distributes their spoils and alms, and they refer disputes and marriages to him, and he oversees matters of Friday and Eid prayers, and he gives justice to the oppressed and takes revenge from the oppressor, and he appoints judges and governors in every region, and he sends out reciters and callers to every frontier.
Source pages p. 477

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal exposition on the imamate. The author begins by stating that the imamate is not among the fundamental principles of belief (usul al-i'tiqad) where investigation would lead to decisive certainty about specific individuals. However, he warns that the danger of erring in this matter is greater than being ignorant of its very basis, and that partisanship from misguided passions prevents objectivity. Next, he reports the consensus of various groups—the Hadith folk from the Ash'aris, jurists, a group of Shi'a, Mu'tazila, and most Khawarij—that the imamate is obligatory (wajib) as a divine command. Then he specifies the Ahl al-Sunna position: it is a communal obligation (fard 'ala al-muslimin) to establish an imam, and obeying the imam (here called 'al-mansur' – the one given victory) is obligatory upon them. He then enumerates the necessary functions of the imam, providing a comprehensive list: executing legal judgments, enforcing hudud punishments, protecting territory, guarding the domain, organizing armies, distributing spoils and zakat, adjudicating disputes and marriages, overseeing Friday and Eid prayers, giving justice to the oppressed, taking justice from the oppressor, appointing judges and governors, and sending da'is and reciters to all regions. This establishes the necessity of an imam for the proper functioning of Islamic society.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Imamate is a core pillar of faith like tawhid and nubuwwa.
Correction: The author explicitly states it is not from usul al-i'tiqad; it is a practical obligation.

Checkpoint
According to the author, which groups affirm that the imamate is obligatory by divine command?
A) Only the Ash'aris and Hanbalis.
B) Only the Shi'a and Mu'tazila.
C) The majority of hadith folk, jurists, a group of Shi'a, Mu'tazila, and most Khawarij.
D) Only Ahl al-Sunna.

Key Takeaway
Imamate is a necessary institution for the community's religious and worldly affairs, though not a fundamental article of faith.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have read that imamate is not from the principles of belief, yet the danger of error in it is greater than ignorance. In your own heart, have you ever given any human leader a level of reverence that belongs only to Allah and His Prophet? How does this passage correct that imbalance?
?
The author lists over a dozen duties of the imam—from executing judgments to sending callers. Look at the Muslim community today: which of these duties are being neglected? What is one concrete step you can take this week to support the establishment of a just and capable leadership?
?
Consider the statement that following the imam is obligatory upon you. Are you resenting or resisting the legitimate authority in your life, or are you praying for its rectification? Be honest: where does your heart stand on obedience to Muslim rulers?

The Proof of Consensus for Imamate
Proves the obligation of imamate by consensus

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the consensus of the early Muslim community serves as a proof for the obligation of the imamate.

Core Idea
The community's unbroken agreement proves that an imam is always necessary.

This passage presents a key argument for the obligation of the imamate: the consensus of the entire Muslim community from the first generation onward. The author cites the speech of Abu Bakr at the Saqifa as evidence that the Companions immediately acted to appoint a leader, showing that the imamate is a required institution.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage is cut off mid-sentence at the end.
2
Identify the proof
The passage states that the 'brilliant proof' is the agreement (consensus) of the community from the first generation to the present. This is a key doctrinal claim.
The proof is based on transmission (sam'an), not rational necessity alone.
3
Examine the example from Abu Bakr
Abu Bakr's sermon shows that the first generation acted immediately to establish an imam. This is presented as evidence of the consensus.
Abu Bakr recited Quran 3:144 to remind that the Prophet died, but the community must continue.
4
Reflect on the strength of consensus
Consider why consensus is a compelling proof in Islamic theology. How does the example of the Companions support the necessity of an imam?
The passage implies that the entire community agreed, making this a binding proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والدليل الساطع على وجوب الإمامة سمعا اتفاق الأمة بأسرهم من الصدر الأول إلى زماننا أن الأرض لا يجوز أن تخلو عن إمام قائم بالأمر. أما الصدر الأول فقد قال أبو بكر رضي الله عنه في خطبته قبل البيعة: أيها الناس من كان يعبد محمدًا فإن محمدًا قد مات، ومن كان يعبد الله فإنه حي لا يموت وتلا هذه الآية: وما محمد إلا رسول قد خلت من قبله الرسل [آل عمران: 144]، ثم قال: وإن محمدا قد مضى بسبيله، ولا بد لهذا الأمر من قائم يقوم به، فانظروا وهاتوا
And the brilliant proof for the obligation of the imamate by transmission is the agreement of the entire community from the first generation to our time that the earth is not permissible to be devoid of an imam who stands with the affair. As for the first generation, Abu Bakr, may Allah be pleased with him, said in his sermon before the pledge of allegiance: 'O people, whoever used to worship Muhammad, then Muhammad has died, and whoever used to worship Allah, then He is alive and does not die,' and he recited this verse: 'And Muhammad is not but a messenger; messengers have passed away before him' [Al 'Imran 3:144]. Then he said: 'And Muhammad has gone his way, and there must be for this affair one who stands with it, so look and bring forth...'
Source pages p. 478

Commentary
This passage presents the proof (dalil) for the obligation of the imamate, specifically from transmission (sam'an). The author states that the brilliant proof is the consensus (ijma') of the entire Muslim community, from the first generation (al-sadr al-awwal) to the present, that the earth cannot be without an imam who takes charge of affairs. To support this, he cites an example from the first generation: the speech of Abu Bakr al-Siddiq before the pledge of allegiance at the Saqifa. Abu Bakr addressed the people, saying that whoever worshipped Muhammad should know that Muhammad has died, but whoever worshipped Allah knows that Allah is eternally alive. He then recited Quran 3:144, which reminds that Muhammad is only a messenger like those before him. Abu Bakr then concluded that Muhammad has passed away, and this affair (al-amr) must have someone to carry it out, urging the people to look and bring forth a candidate. This historical event demonstrates the immediate consensus of the Companions on the necessity of establishing an imam after the Prophet's death, thereby proving the obligation of the imamate. The proof is based on the unbroken practice of the community.

الصدر الأول
إمام قائم بالأمر
أبو بكر
بيعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that the imamate is not a necessary institution and that the community could function without a leader.
Correction: The passage argues that consensus proves it is necessary, and the immediate action of the Companions refutes this misconception.

Checkpoint
What is the 'brilliant proof' (al-dalil al-sati') for the obligation of the imamate according to the author?
A) An explicit Quranic verse.
B) A hadith from the Prophet.
C) The consensus of the community from the first generation onward.
D) Rational necessity alone.

Key Takeaway
The immediate appointment of a leader after the Prophet's death, as exemplified by Abu Bakr's sermon, shows the consensus of the Companions on the necessity of the imamate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you were among the Companions at the Saqifa, would you have felt the same urgency to appoint a leader, or would you have waited? What does your honest answer reveal about your attachment to the Prophet versus your commitment to the community?
?
The consensus of the community is presented as a brilliant proof. But do you personally rely on the collective guidance of the Muslim community in your own life, or do you tend to go it alone? What is one decision you can make today to align yourself with the community's agreed-upon principles?
?
Abu Bakr redirected the people from worshipping Muhammad to worshipping Allah alone. Is there any person, leader, or figure in your life whose presence you value so much that you would struggle to see the continuity of Allah's command without them? How can you internalize that the affair (al-amr) continues with or without any individual?

The Sunni Proof Against Nass for the Imam
Author presents Sunni argument against nass and for ijma' in appointing the Imam

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni argument against the existence of a definitive text (nass) appointing a specific Imam and the logical basis for relying on consensus (ijma').

Core Idea
If a nass existed, it would be universally known — but it is not.

The question of how the Imam is appointed is central to Islamic political theology. Some groups claimed the Prophet explicitly named a successor, while the majority Sunni position holds that the Imam is chosen by consensus. In this passage, the author lays out the points of disagreement and then presents a rigorous proof to refute the existence of any explicit text. This argument is based on the principle of tawatur: if such a critical text existed, it would have been massively transmitted and known to all, yet history shows no such knowledge. Follow along as the author builds his case.

Guided Path
1
Understand the classifications
The author first summarises the different positions: those who claim a text (nass) vs. those who say consensus (ijma'), and sub-splits among them. Note the sub-disagreements: whether the text names a specific person or describes him, and whether consensus must be unanimous or just from the people of binding and loosing.
This sets up the precise targets of the argument.
2
Grasp the Sunni proof structure
The proof is a conditional: if a nass for a specific Imam existed, it would be mutawatir and obligate every accountable person. Since no such obligation is felt, the antecedent is false.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.
3
Examine the custom-based argument
The author adds that it is impossible (muhal) as a matter of customary circumstances for such a transmission to be lost, given the pressing need and the early Muslim community’s conditions.
This reinforces the logical proof with historical plausibility.
4
Relate to broader theological context
Consider why this argument matters: Sunni political legitimacy rests on community consensus rather than a single divinely appointed figure. This shapes Sunni understandings of leadership and authority.
This is a foundational argument in Sunni kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما القول في تعيين الإمام ؛ هل (۳) هو ثابت بالنص أم بالإجماع؟ فالقائلون اختلفوا في أن النص ورد على شخص بعينه، أم ورد بذكر صفته)، فالقائلون بالإجماع اختلفوا في أن إجماع الأمة عن بكرة أبيهم شرط في ثبوت الإمامة، أم يكتفى بجماعة من أهل الحل والعقد، وقد ذكرت مذاهبهم في الكتب. قال أهل السنة القائلون بالإجماع : الدليل على عدم النص على إمام بعينه هو أنه لو ورد نص على إمام بعينه لكانت الأمة بأسرهم مكلفين بطاعته ولا سبيل لهم إلى العلم بعينه بأدلة العقول، والخبر لو (۱) كان تواتراً لكان كل مكلف يجد من نفسه العلم بوجوب الطاعة له وإلا لزمه (۷) دينًا كما لزمه الصلوات الخمس دينا ، ولما جازوا إلى غيره بيعة وإجماع. ومن المحال من حيث العادة أن يسمع الجم الغفير كلاما من رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم ثم لا ينقلونه في مظنة الحاجة وعصيان الأمة بمخالفته، والدواعي بالضرورة تتوفر على النقل خصوصاً وهم في نأناة الإسلام وطراوة (۱) الدين، وصفوة القلوب، وخلوص العقائد عن الضغائن والأحقاد والتآلف المذكور في ...
As for the discussion concerning the appointment of the Imam: is it established by a definitive text (nass) or by consensus (ijma')? The proponents differed: [some said] that the text came for a specific person, or that it came mentioning his description. Those who [hold] consensus differed: whether the consensus of the entire community, every one of them, is a condition for establishing the imamate, or whether a group of the people of binding and loosing (ahl al-hall wa al-'aqd) is sufficient. I have mentioned their schools in the books. The Ahl al-Sunna who hold consensus said: the proof for the absence of a definitive text for a specific Imam is that if a text had come for a specific Imam, then the entire community would be obligated to obey him, and they would have no way to know him by rational proofs. And if the report were mutawatir, every obligated person would find within himself knowledge of the obligation of obedience to him, otherwise it would be binding on him as religion, just as the five daily prayers are binding as religion. And they would not turn to another for allegiance and consensus. And it is impossible, as a matter of custom, that a large multitude hear speech from the Messenger of God, may God bless him and grant him peace, and then not transmit it in a situation of need and [to avoid] the disobedience of the community by opposing it. The motives of necessity would inevitably arise for transmission, especially as they were in the dawn of Islam, the freshness of the religion, the purity of hearts, the sincerity of beliefs free from grudges and rancor, and the harmony mentioned in ...
Source pages p. 480

Commentary
This passage is a classification and argument about the method of appointing the Imam. The author first delineates the points of disagreement: (1) Is the Imam appointed by a definitive text (nass) from the Prophet, or by consensus (ijma')? Among those who say by text, they differ whether the text names a specific person or only describes him. Among those who say by consensus, they differ whether the consensus must be unanimous or merely that of the people of binding and loosing (ahl al-hall wa al-'aqd). Then the author presents the Sunni position: those who say by ijma' provide a proof (dalil) that there is no text for a specific Imam. The proof is a reductio ad absurdum: If such a text existed, it would be a legal obligation for the entire community to obey that specific person. However, they have no way of knowing him definitively through rational proofs (adillat al-'uqul). If the report of his appointment were mutawatir (massively transmitted), then every morally responsible person would have immediate knowledge of his obligation to obey that Imam, just as the obligation of the five daily prayers is known. But since the community did not have such knowledge and instead turned to others for allegiance and consensus, the absence of such a text is proven. Furthermore, it is contrary to customary circumstances (muhal min hayth al-'ada) that a large multitude would hear a clear statement from the Prophet on a matter of such importance and fail to transmit it, especially when the community's necessity and the danger of disobedience demand its preservation. The historical context—the early period of Islam, with fresh faith, pure hearts, and lack of rancor—makes it even more implausible that such a text would be suppressed. The passage ends abruptly at the page break, but the argument is essentially complete.

Key Terms
نص
إجماع
تعيين الإمام
تواتر
أهل الحل والعقد
أهل السنة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Sunni position on ijma' in imamate is purely political without theological foundation.
Correction: The author provides a rigorous theological proof from tawatur and custom, showing it is a matter of scriptural and rational evidence.

Checkpoint
According to the Sunni argument in this passage, why is it impossible that a definitive text (nass) for a specific Imam existed?
A) The Companions were forbidden from recording it
B) If it existed, it would be mutawatir and known to all, but it was not
C) The Quran does not mention any Imam
D) The Prophet never mentioned anyone by name

Key Takeaway
The Sunni argument against a definitive text for the Imam relies on the logical principle that such a text would be mutawatir and universally known, but history shows no such transmission, so it did not exist.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear a claim about an obligation in religion that is not widely known or transmitted, do you accept it without question? This lesson shows that a truly binding divine command would be universally known. Are there practices you follow that you have not verified through widespread authentic transmission? Will you investigate them?
?
The early Muslims transmitted everything because of the freshness of faith and purity of hearts. How does your own spiritual state affect your commitment to preserving and transmitting Islamic knowledge? Are you a reliable link in the chain? What do you need to change to become more sincere?
?
If you were part of the community after the Prophet, would you have accepted the consensus of the people of binding and loosing, or would you have demanded a text? This lesson argues that the lack of a text is itself a proof. How does this challenge your desire for direct textual authority in matters of leadership? Can you trust the collective wisdom of the qualified scholars? Will you submit to ijma' even when it contradicts your personal inclination?

The Appointment of the Imam: Text vs. Consensus
Classifies opinions on imam appointment by text or consensus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the different opinions among Muslims regarding how the imam is appointed, specifically whether by explicit text or by consensus, and the further subdivisions within each view.

Core Idea
The appointment of the imam is a disputed question, not a settled issue.

The author begins by framing the central question of how the imam is appointed. Is it by a direct textual designation or by the consensus of the community? This seemingly simple question splits into multiple positions, each with its own nuances. The passage categorizes these positions to prepare for a detailed discussion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the English translation to grasp the overall classification.
Notice how the author poses the main question and then immediately subdivides each answer.
2
Identify the two main camps
The passage divides opinions into two groups: those who hold that the imam is appointed by text (nass) and those who hold by consensus (ijma).
3
Explore the sub-disagreements
Within each camp, there are further disputes. For the text camp: does the text name a specific person or only describe qualities? For the consensus camp: is the consensus of the entire community required or just that of the people of binding and loosing?
These subdivisions show that even within a single broad position, there is significant variation.
4
Consider why this classification matters
Think about how this classification sets the stage for the author's own argument. By mapping the landscape of opinions, he prepares to refute some and defend others.
This is a common strategy in kalam: first lay out the views, then critique them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما القول في تعيين الإمام ; هل هو ثابت بالنص أم بالإجماع؟ فالقائلون اختلفوا في أن النص ورد على شخص بعينه، أم ورد بذكر صفته، فالقائلون بالإجماع اختلفوا في أن إجماع الأمة عن بكرة أبيهم شرط في ثبوت الإمامة، أم يكتفى بجماعة من أهل الحل والعقد، وقد ذكرت مذاهبهم في الكتب.
As for the statement regarding the appointment of the Imam: is it established by text or by consensus? The proponents differed whether the text specified a particular person, or mentioned his description. Those who hold by consensus differed whether the consensus of the entire community from their first to their last is a condition for the establishment of the imamate, or whether a group of the people of solution and contract is sufficient. And I have mentioned their schools in the books.
Source pages p. 480

Commentary
This passage classifies the differing opinions regarding the appointment of the imam. The author first states the main question: Is the imam appointed by explicit text (nass) or by consensus (ijma)? Then he notes further subdivisions: Among those who say by text, there is disagreement on whether the text names a specific person or only describes his qualities. Among those who say by consensus, there is disagreement on whether the consensus of the entire community is required or only that of the people of binding and loosing (ahl al-hall wa al-'aqd). The author then remarks that he has discussed these schools in other books. The purpose of this classification is to set up the discussion that follows, where Ahl al-Sunna argue against the existence of an explicit text.

Key Terms

أهل الحل والعقد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many assume that the appointment of the imam is agreed upon by all Muslims, especially Shia and Sunni.
Correction: This passage shows that even among Muslims, there are fundamental disagreements on how the imam is appointed, with multiple interpretations within each major camp.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what are the two main areas of disagreement regarding the appointment of the imam?
A) Whether the imam must be from Quraysh, and whether he must be just.
B) Whether the appointment is by text or consensus, and within each, further differences.
C) Whether the imam must be learned or not.
D) Whether the imam must be chosen by the entire community or only by the scholars.

Key Takeaway
The imam's appointment is a contested issue with multiple positions, which the author classifies to set up his own argument.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about religious leadership, are you more comfortable with an explicit, textually defined authority or with a process of community consensus? Which feels safer to you, and what does that reveal about your reliance on God versus your trust in the community?
?
The passage shows deep disagreement among early Muslims over how an imam is appointed. Have you ever taken the time to understand these differences, or do you rely on a single inherited opinion without investigation? What would it cost you to truly learn the other views?
?
Only a select group (ahl al-hall wa al-aqd) is considered sufficient for consensus by some. In your own life, do you seek guidance from those qualified to give it, or do you act as if every personal opinion carries equal weight? How will you start submitting to proper authority in matters you are not expert in?

Proof That No Specific Imam Was Designated by Text
Ahl al-Sunna present three proofs for no explicit text.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand three key arguments used by Ahl al-Sunna to demonstrate that no explicit text from the Prophet designated a specific imam.

Core Idea
No explicit text named an imam—three rational proofs confirm it.

Ahl al-Sunna who advocate for consensus as the basis for imamate provide a multi-layered argument that the Prophet did not designate a specific person as his successor. Instead of a text, the community's consensus became the legitimate source. This passage presents the core proofs that support this view, examining what would have happened if a text had existed and why its absence is conclusive.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the English translation to grasp the overall flow of the argument.
Notice the logical structure: the passage uses conditional reasoning and appeals to historical impossibility.
2
Identify the three proofs
As you read, note the three distinct arguments: the conditional obligation argument, the transmission argument, and the behavior of the designated person argument.
The first argument is based on necessary knowledge (mutawatir) and community obligation.
3
Understand 'motives for transmission' (دواعي النقل)
Focus on the second proof: the claim that it is impossible for a large crowd to hear a crucial text and not transmit it, given strong motives and absence of deterrents.
This principle is central to the method of discerning historical reports.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this argument is foundational for the Sunni position on the caliphate. How does it shift the basis of legitimacy from text to consensus?
The passage refutes claims of explicit designation and supports the role of community consensus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أهل السنة القائلون بالإجماع : الدليل على عدم النص على إمام بعينه هو أنه لو ورد نص على إمام بعينه لكانت الأمة بأسرهم مكلفين بطاعته ولا سبيل لهم إلى العلم بعينه بأدلة العقول، والخبر لو كان تواتراً لكان كل مكلف يجد من نفسه العلم بوجوب الطاعة له وإلا لزمه دينًا كما لزمه الصلوات الخمس دينا، ولما جازوا إلى غيره بيعة وإجماع. ومن المحال من حيث العادة أن يسمع الجم الغفير كلاما من رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم ثم لا ينقلونه في مظنة الحاجة وعصيان الأمة بمخالفته، والدواعي بالضرورة تتوفر على النقل خصوصاً وهم في نأناة الإسلام وطراوة الدين، وصفوة القلوب، وخلوص العقائد عن الضغائن والأحقاد والتآلف المذكور في الكتاب العزيز : ( وألف بين قلوبكم فأصبحتم بنعمته إخوانا ) [آل عمران: 10]، وإذا كانت الدواعي على النقل موجودة والصوارف عنه مفقودة، ولم ينقل دل على أنه لم يكن في الباب نص أصلا، وأيضًا لو عين شخصا لكان يجب على ذلك الشخص المعين أن يتحدى بالإمامة ويخاصم عليها ويخوض فيها، حتى إذا دفع عن حقه سكت ولزم بيته فيظهر الظالم عليه، ولم ينقل أن أحدا تصدى للإمامة وادعاها نصا عليه وتسليما إليه.
The people of the Sunna who hold by consensus said: The proof for the absence of a text on a specific imam is that if a text had come down on a specific imam, the entire community would be obligated to obey him, and they would have no means to know his identity by rational proofs. And if the report were mutawatir, every obligated person would find in himself knowledge of the obligation to obey him, otherwise it would be incumbent on him as a religious duty just as the five prayers are incumbent as a religious duty, and they would not have turned to someone else by pledge and consensus. And it is impossible, from the perspective of normal experience, that a great multitude heard speech from the Messenger of God, peace be upon him, and then did not transmit it in a context of need and the sin of the community in opposing him. The motives for transmission are necessarily present, especially while they were in the freshness of Islam and the newness of the religion, and the purity of hearts, and the sincerity of beliefs free from grudges and rancor, and the harmony mentioned in the Mighty Book: 'And He brought your hearts together, so you became, by His grace, brothers' [Quran 3:103]. And since the motives for transmission existed and the deterrents were absent, and it was not transmitted, this indicates that there was no text at all in the matter. Also, if he had designated a person, that designated person would have had to claim the imamate and dispute over it and plunge into it, so that if he was denied his right, he would remain silent and stay in his house, and thus the wrongdoer would appear victorious over him. And it is not reported that anyone put himself forward for the imamate and claimed it by a text and surrender to it.
Source pages p. 480

Commentary
This passage presents the proof of Ahl al-Sunna (those who hold by consensus) that there was no explicit text designating a specific imam. The proof is structured as a series of arguments: First, if there were a text naming a specific imam, the entire community would be obligated to obey him. But they would have no way to identify him using rational proofs. If the report were mutawatir, every obligated person would necessarily find in themselves the knowledge that obedience is required, just as they know the obligation of the five daily prayers. In that case, they would not have turned to someone else by pledge and consensus. Second, it is contrary to normal human experience that a large crowd would hear a statement from the Prophet and then fail to transmit it, especially when there is a clear need and the sin of the community in opposing him. The motives for transmission were strong, particularly in the early period of Islam when faith was fresh, hearts were pure, and beliefs were free from grudges. The Quran itself mentions the harmony among believers (Quran 3:103). Since the motives existed and the deterrents were absent, and the text was not transmitted, this proves that no such text existed. Third, if a specific person had been designated, that person would have claimed the imamate and argued for it. If he were denied his right, he would not have remained silent and stayed at home, letting the oppressor prevail. But history does not report that anyone claimed the imamate by explicit text and surrender to it. Thus, the conclusion is that no explicit text naming a specific imam ever existed.

Key Terms
نص
إمام
تواتر
بيعة
إجماع
دواعي النقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that hadiths like 'I leave among you the Book of Allah and my family' are explicit texts for imamate.
Correction: The passage argues that such statements are not explicit texts naming a specific person, and the absence of transmission proves they were not.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the lack of transmission of a text naming a specific imam prove that no such text existed?
A) Because the need was so great that transmission would have been inevitable.
B) Because the Prophet forbade writing down his speech.
C) Because the community was unanimous in rejecting imamate.
D) Because the imam's identity was known by intuition.

Key Takeaway
The absence of a transmitted text naming an imam, given the strong motives for transmission, demonstrates that no such text ever existed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the early community, with their pure hearts and harmony, would not have failed to transmit a text naming an imam, why do you still find yourself drawn to claims of exclusive leadership that lack such clear transmission? What does that say about your desire for certainty over truth?
?
The proof argues that a designated person would have risen to claim his right—but no one did. Have you ever stayed silent when you should have spoken up for truth, allowing falsehood to appear victorious? Will you commit to not remaining passive when truth demands your voice?
?
You rely on the consensus of the community for your understanding of leadership. But do you truly submit to that consensus even when it challenges your personal preferences or attachments? Examine your heart: is there any leader or sect you cling to despite the absence of explicit text and despite the community's agreed practice?

Objection: Imamate Is Not Obligatory
The Najadat and some Qadariyya argue that imamate is not a religious obligation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key arguments used by the Najadat and some Qadariyya to deny the obligation of imamate, and the logical structure of their objection.

Core Idea
Imamate is challenged as unnecessary; cooperation and equality replace hierarchy.

In this passage, we encounter a major objection to the Sunni doctrine of imamate from the Khawarij and Qadariyya. They argue that imamate is not a religious obligation but a matter of mutual convenience. Their reasoning relies on the equality of all mujtahids and the sufficiency of collective piety. As we read, notice the analogies they employ and the incomplete closing argument about scriptural proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and its translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to grasp the overall argument. Pay attention to the sectarian attribution.
The passage is incomplete; the final argument cuts off.
2
Identify the central claim
Find the statement that imamate is not obligatory in the sense of incurring blame or punishment if abandoned.
This directly challenges the Ash'ari position that imamate is wajib.
3
List the supporting reasons
The passage gives several reasons: imamate is based on transactions; cooperation makes an imam unnecessary; all mujtahids are equal; analogies of comb teeth and a herd without a riding camel; and the lack of a clear text.
These reasons are meant to show that obedience to a superior is groundless.
4
Analyze the analogies
Examine the two analogies: 'people are like the teeth of a comb' (equality) and 'like a herd of one hundred with no riding camel' (no distinct leader). What point are they making?
These are rhetorical tools to deny any inherent superiority.
5
Note the incomplete argument
The passage ends mid-sentence: 'the obligation of obedience to one person ... is either established by a text from the Messenger, and you have proven that there is no text on anyone, or by ...' The conclusion is missing.
In context, the objection likely concludes that since no text exists, no imamate is obligatory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالت النجدات من الخوارج وجماعة من القدرية مثل أبي بكر الأصم وهشام الفوطي : إن الإمامة غير واجبة في الشرع وجوبا لو امتنعت الأمة عن ذلك استحقوا اللوم والعقاب، بل هي مبنية على معاملات الناس، فإن تعادلوا وتعاونوا وتناصروا على البر والتقوى، واشتغل كل واحد من المكلفين بواجبه وتكليفه استغنوا عن الإمام ومتابعته، فإن كل واحد من المجتهدين مثل صاحبه في الدين والإسلام والعلم والاجتهاد والناس كأسنان المشط، والناس كابل ماية لا تجد فيها راحلة، فمن أين يلزم وجوب الطاعة لمن هو مثله ؟ وزادوا على ذلك تقريرا بأن قالوا : وجوب الطاعة لواحد الأمة إما أن يثبت بنص من الرسول، فقد دللتم على أنه لا نص على أحد، وإما أن
The Najadat from the Khawarij and a group from the Qadariyya, such as Abu Bakr al-Asamm and Hisham al-Fuwti, said: The imamate is not obligatory in the Law in such a way that if the community refrained from it they would deserve blame and punishment. Rather, it is based on the transactions of people. For if they treat each other equitably and cooperate and support each other in righteousness and piety, and each of the obligated persons occupies himself with his duty and responsibility, they become independent of the imam and following him. For each of the mujtahids is like his companion in religion, Islam, knowledge, and ijtihad. And people are like the teeth of a comb, and people are like a herd of one hundred that has no riding camel in it. So from where does the obligation of obedience to one who is like him arise? And they added to that by way of consolidation: The obligation of obedience to one person of the community is either established by a text from the Messenger, and you have proven that there is no text on anyone, or by
Source pages p. 481

Commentary
This passage presents an objection to the necessity of imamate from the Najadat sect of the Khawarij and some Qadariyya, including Abu Bakr al-Asamm and Hisham al-Fuwti. They argue that imamate is not obligatory in the sense that the community would be blamed and punished for abandoning it. Instead, it is a matter of mutual dealings (mu'amalat). If people treat each other justly, cooperate in righteousness and piety, and each individual fulfills his own duties, they have no need for an imam to follow. They claim that all mujtahids are equal in religion, Islam, knowledge, and ijtihad. They use analogies: people are likened to the teeth of a comb (equal in rank), and to a herd of one hundred camels with no riding camel among them (i.e., no one is superior). Thus, how can obedience be required to someone who is one's equal? They then reinforce this by stating that the obligation to obey one person must come from a text of the Prophet, and since Ahl al-Sunna have proven that no such text exists, the obligation cannot be established by consensus either (though the text cuts off here). This objection challenges the very basis of imamate as a religious obligation.

Key Terms
إمامة
وجوب
معاملات
مجتهد
مشط
راحلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Imamate is not an obligatory duty; people can live without an imam through mutual cooperation and equality.
Correction: According to Sunni consensus, imamate is obligatory (wajib) by revelation and reason; the community is blameworthy if they fail to appoint an imam.

Checkpoint
According to the Najadat, why would people not need an imam?
A) Because the imam must be from Quraysh.
B) Because if people cooperate and fulfill their duties, they can do without an imam.
C) Because the imam is always unjust.
D) Because the Prophet did not command imamate.

Key Takeaway
The Najadat and Qadariyya deny the obligation of imamate, arguing that equality and mutual cooperation render a leader unnecessary, and that no scriptural text mandates obedience to one person.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever resist submitting to legitimate authority in your life (in your family, community, or mosque) because you see yourself as equal to those over you? What would it cost you to humbly follow even when you feel you could lead?
?
If you truly believed that no leader is necessary for the community, would your practical commitment to the unity and organization of the Muslim community change? Examine your heart: do you value independence more than obedience?
?
The Najadat argued that equality makes obedience unnecessary. But is there someone in your life whom you are called to obey for the sake of Allah, even if they are your equal in faith? What holds you back from that obedience?

The Absence of Nass for Imamah
The author argues that absence of transmitted nass proves no divine appointment for imamah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational argument from the absence of transmitted reports to prove that the imamah was not divinely appointed by explicit text (nass).

Core Idea
If a divine text for imamah existed, it would have been transmitted; its absence proves it never existed.

This passage presents a powerful historical and rational argument used by Sunni theologians against the Shia claim of an explicit designation for the imamah. The author employs two interconnected proofs: the principle of transmission under normal motives, and the observable behavior of claimants to power. By reading this, you will see how absence of evidence can become evidence of absence in theological reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the use of a Quranic verse as a starting point.
2
Understand the first argument: transmission condition
The author states that if motives for transmission (dawa'i) are strong and deterrents (sawarif) are absent, but no transmission occurred, then the claim of a nass is false. Reflect on why this logical inference holds.
3
Analyze the second argument: behavior of a designated person
The author hypothesizes what a truly designated imam would do: he would actively claim and fight for his right, not remain silent. Since no such claim is reported, no designation existed.
4
Connect the two arguments
Both arguments rely on observable facts (lack of transmission, lack of claim) to disprove an unseen alleged event (nass). Consider how this method is used in kalam.
5
Reflect on significance
This argument rebuts the core Shia doctrine of nass. Think about its strengths and possible objections.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكتاب العزيز: {وألف بين قلوبكم فأصبحتم بنعمته إخوانا} [آل عمران: 103]، وإذا كانت الدواعي على النقل موجودة والصوارف عنه مفقودة، ولم ينقل دل على أنه لم يكن في الباب نص أصلا، وأيضا لو عين شخصا لكان يجب على ذلك الشخص المعين أن يتحدى بالإمامة ويخاصم عليها ويخوض فيها، حتى إذا دفع عن حقه سكت ولزم بيته فيظهر الظالم عليه، ولم ينقل أن أحدا تصدى للإمامة وادعاها نصا عليه وتسليما إليه.
The Noble Book: {And He united your hearts, so you became by His grace brothers} [Al Imran: 103], and if the motives for transmission exist and the deterrents from it are absent, and yet it was not transmitted, then that indicates that there was no text in the matter at all. And also, if He had designated a specific person, then that designated person would have to claim the imamah, dispute over it, and engage in it, until when his right is repelled, he would keep silent and stay in his house, so the oppressor would prevail over him. And it was not transmitted that anyone sought the imamah and claimed it by text and submission to him.
Source pages p. 481

Commentary
This passage is an argument against the claim that the imamah was divinely appointed by explicit text (nass). The author begins by citing the Quranic verse about unity (Al Imran 103) to underscore the communal nature of leadership. He then employs two logical arguments: First, if there were an explicit text from God or the Prophet designating a specific person for the imamah, the motives for transmitting it would have been strong (dawa'i) and the deterrents (sawarif) weak or absent. Since no such transmission has come down to us, it demonstrates that no such text existed. Second, if a person had been explicitly designated, that person would have been expected to publicly claim the imamah, dispute with opponents, and actively seek rightful dominion. But history shows that no one ever came forth claiming the imamah on the basis of a clear text and submission to him. Therefore, the absence of such claims proves that no nass existed.

Key Terms
النص (al-nass)
الإمامة (al-imamah)
الدواعي (al-dawa'i)
الصوارف (al-sawarif)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some claim that the nass for imamah existed but was suppressed or lost.
Correction: The author argues that strong motives for transmission make suppression unlikely; the absence of transmission proves no nass existed.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the absence of a report about someone claiming imamah by nass prove that no nass exists?
A) Because the designated person would have remained silent.
B) Because motives for transmission would be present but no transmission occurred.
C) Because the Quran prohibits claiming imamah.
D) Because the community would have rejected it.

Key Takeaway
The absence of transmitted reports about a nass for imamah, combined with strong motives for transmission, proves that no such nass ever existed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held a belief about religious leadership without a clear textual basis? If the evidence is absent despite strong motives for its preservation, are you willing to re-examine that belief today?
?
The designated person would have spoken up and claimed his right. When you see injustice or falsehood in your community, do you remain silent like the one who 'kept silent and stayed in his house'? What right are you not claiming that belongs to you as a believer?
?
If the absence of a transmitted text can prove it never existed, what personal assumptions about Islam are you holding that may crumble under the same logical test? Will you test them now, or wait until the Day when all hidden things are made manifest?

The Najdat and Qadari Denial of the Obligation of Imamah
The Najdat and Qadariyyah argue against the obligation of imamah and the possibility of unanimous choice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Kharijite and Qadari argument that imamah is not a religious obligation and that unanimous choice for a leader is impossible.

Core Idea
The Najdat and Qadariyyah deny imamah's necessity, arguing people can manage without a ruler.

This lesson presents the position of two early Islamic sects—the Najdat (a Kharijite group) and the Qadariyyah—who argued that establishing an imam is not a religious obligation. They base their view on the idea that mutual cooperation suffices and that no valid basis exists for requiring obedience to a single leader. The passage also uses the historical example of the first caliphate to demonstrate the impossibility of unanimous agreement.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without interruption, then consult the translation. Notice the two main claims: that imamah is not obligatory, and that unanimous choice is impossible.
The passage includes a detailed historical argument from the Saqifa incident.
2
Identify the two lines of argument
First, the Najdat and Qadariyyah claim that imamah is a practical matter, not a religious duty. Second, they argue that even if it were obligatory, unanimous choice (ijma') is impossible because ijtihad leads to differing opinions.
3
Examine the historical evidence
The passage cites the events of Saqifa, the pledge of Abu Bakr, the withdrawal of Banu Umayya and Banu Hashim, and Abu Sufyan's temptation of Ali. Notice how these events are used to show that consensus was not achieved even among the best generation.
The phrase 'فلتة' (sudden affair) is key to their argument.
4
Reflect on the challenge to Sunni doctrine
Consider how this argument questions the Sunni view that imamah is obligatory and based on consensus. The response in Sunni kalam will need to address these points.
This passage shows the importance of historical precedents in theological debates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالت النجدات من الخوارج وجماعة من القدرية مثل أبي بكر الأصم وهشام الفوطي: إن الإمامة غير واجبة في الشرع وجوبا لو امتنعت الأمة عن ذلك استحقوا اللوم والعقاب، بل هي مبنية على معاملات الناس، فإن تعادلوا وتعاونوا وتناصروا على البر والتقوى، واشتغل كل واحد من المكلفين بواجبه وتكليفه استغنوا عن الإمام ومتابعته، فإن كل واحد من المجتهدين مثل صاحبه في الدين والإسلام والعلم والاجتهاد والناس كأسنان المشط، والناس كابل ماية لا تجد فيها راحلة، فمن أين يلزم وجوب الطاعة لمن هو مثله؟ وزادوا على ذلك تقريرا بأن قالوا: وجوب الطاعة لواحد الأمة إما أن يثبت بنص من الرسول، فقد دللتم على أنه لا نص على أحد، وإما أن يكون باختيار من المجتهدين والاختيار من كل واحد من الأمة إجماعا، بحيث لا يقدر فيه اختلاف لا عقلا ولا وقوعا، أما العقل فإن الاختيار إذا كان مبنيا على الاجتهاد والاجتهاد ينبني على ما تعين لكل واحد من العقلاء من قضايا تردده في الوجوه العقلية والسمعية، وذلك إذا كان مختلفا في الطباع فبالضرورة أن مختلفا في الحكم، أليس أحق الأحكام بوجود الاتفاق فيه الخلافة الأولى؟ وأولى الأزمان في الشرع هو الزمان الأول؟ وأولى الأشخاص بالصدق والإخلاص الصحابة؟ وأحق الصحابة بالأمانة ونفي التهمة والخيانة المهاجرون والأنصار؟ وأقرب الناس إلى رسول الله أبو بكر وعمر؟ فانظر كيف انحاز الأنصار إلى السقيفة وكيف قالوا: منا أمير ومنكم أمير وكيف أجمعوا على سعد بن عبادة لولا أن تداركه عمر بأن بايع بنفسه حتى شايعه الناس، ثم قال بعد ذلك: ألا إن بيعة أبي بكر كانت فلتة فوقى الله شرها، فمن عاد إلى مثلها فاقتلوه. فأيما رجل بايع رجلا من غير مشورة من المسلمين فإنها أن يقتلا؛ يعني أني بايعت أبا بكر وما شاروت الجماعة، ووقى الله شرها فلا تعودوا إلى مثلها. ولم يكن وقت البيعة اتفاق الجماعة وفي الغد لما بايعوه انحازت بنو أمية وبنو هاشم حتى قال أبو سفيان لعلي رضي الله عنه: لم تدع هذا الأمر يكون في شر قبيلة من قريش، فأجابه علي: فتنتنا وأنت كافر، وتريد أن تغره حتى...
The Najdat of the Kharijites and a group of the Qadariyyah such as Abu Bakr al-Asamm and Hisham al-Fuwati said: The imamah is not obligatory in the law in such a way that if the community refrained from it they would deserve blame and punishment. Rather, it is based on the mutual dealings of people. If they treat each other equitably, cooperate, and support each other in righteousness and piety, and each one of the responsible individuals occupies himself with his duty and obligation, they are independent of the imam and following him. For each one of the mujtahids is like his companion in religion, submission, knowledge, and ijtihad. People are like the teeth of a comb; people are like a hundred camels in which you do not find a mount. So from where does the obligation of obedience to one who is like him arise? They added to that by expanding and saying: The obligation of obedience to one person of the community is either established by a text from the Messenger, but you have proven that there is no text for anyone; or it is by choice of the mujtahids, and the choice from each one of the community is a consensus (ijma') such that no difference can be imagined in it, neither rationally nor de facto. As for reason, if choice is based on ijtihad, and ijtihad is based on what is determined for each rational person from issues of hesitation in rational and revealed aspects, then if they differ in nature, necessarily they differ in judgment. Is not the most rightful judgment for agreement upon it the first caliphate? And the most fitting time in the law is the first time? And the most fitting people for truthfulness and sincerity are the Companions? And the most rightful of the Companions for trustworthiness and absolution from accusation and betrayal are the Emigrants and the Helpers? And the closest people to the Messenger of God are Abu Bakr and 'Umar? So look how the Helpers withdrew to the Saqifa and how they said: 'A leader from us and a leader from you' and how they agreed upon Sa'd ibn 'Ubadah, were it not that 'Umar forestalled him by himself giving the pledge of allegiance until the people followed him. Then he said afterwards: 'Indeed, the pledge of allegiance of Abu Bakr was a sudden affair; God protected you from its evil. Whoever returns to something like it, kill him.' Whenever any man gives allegiance to another without consultation of the Muslims, then it is intended that both be killed. Meaning: I gave allegiance to Abu Bakr and I did not consult the community, and God protected from its evil, so do not return to something like it. And there was not at the time of the allegiance agreement of the community, and the next day when they gave allegiance, the Banu Umayya and Banu Hashim withdrew, until Abu Sufyan said to 'Ali, may God be pleased with him: 'Do you leave this matter to be in the worst tribe of Quraysh?' So 'Ali answered him: 'You tempted us while you were an unbeliever, and you want to deceive him until...'
Source pages p. 481

Commentary
This passage presents the position of the Najdat (a Kharijite subgroup) and some Qadariyyah regarding the imamah. They begin by asserting that establishing an imam is not a religious obligation that would incur blame and punishment if neglected. Instead, they view it as a practical arrangement based on mutual cooperation. If people behave equitably and each fulfills his own duties, they have no need for a leader to whom obedience is owed, because all people are equal in religious status and capability. They then strengthen their argument by examining the basis for the obligation to obey one person. They propose two possible sources: either a specific text (nass) from the Prophet designating someone, but they note that the Sunni opponents have already admitted there is no such nass; or it must arise from the unanimous choice of the mujtahids. However, they argue that unanimous choice is impossible because ijtihad relies on individual reasoning, which naturally differs from person to person. To prove this, they point to historical events surrounding the first caliphate (the khilafa al-ula). They note that even among the best people—the Companions, especially the Emigrants and Helpers, and the two most prominent, Abu Bakr and 'Umar—there was disagreement, as shown by the events of Saqifa: the Helpers proposed their own leader (Sa'd ibn 'Ubadah), and 'Umar's quick pledge to Abu Bakr was described as a 'falta' (sudden affair). Moreover, the next day, Banu Umayya and Banu Hashim withheld their allegiance, with Abu Sufyan even suggesting to 'Ali that he should claim the caliphate. 'Ali rebuked him, indicating that Abu Sufyan's suggestion was a temptation from a former unbeliever. Thus, the historical record demonstrates that unanimous agreement was not achieved even in the earliest and most virtuous generation, proving the impossibility of establishing obligatory obedience through unanimous choice. Therefore, the claim that imamah is obligatory is unfounded.

Key Terms
النجدات (al-najdat)
الخوارج (al-khawarij)
القدرية (al-qadariyyah)
الإمامة (al-imamah)
الاجتهاد (al-ijtihad)
الإجماع (al-ijma')

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Najdat and Qadariyyah, why is unanimous choice impossible as a basis for the obligation of imamah?
A) Because ijtihad leads to differing judgments due to natural differences in human nature.
B) Because the Prophet forbade it.
C) Because the Quran requires a text.
D) Because the community is too large.

Key Takeaway
The Najdat and Qadariyyah reject the obligation of imamah by arguing that mutual cooperation suffices and that unanimous agreement on a leader is impossible due to natural differences in human reasoning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The Najdat argued that if everyone fulfills their duties, no leader is needed. Is there an area of your life where you resist accountability by claiming you can manage on your own, while secretly avoiding someone's authority?

The Saqifa event shows that even the best of people had disagreement. When you face conflict in choosing whom to follow, do you use the confusion as an excuse to follow your own opinion rather than seeking the truth with humility? What would change if you truly believed that Allah has not left you without guidance?

Argument against the possibility of consensus
Quotes an argument that consensus is impossible due to differing ijtihad and historical examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational and historical arguments used by some scholars to deny the possibility of consensus (ijma') as a valid proof in Islamic law.

Core Idea
If ijtihad differs by nature, consensus is impossible.

This lesson presents a classical argument against the possibility of consensus. The argument proceeds in two stages: a rational demonstration that agreement is impossible given natural differences in ijtihad, and a historical case study from the election of Abu Bakr at Saqifa. Understanding this argument is essential for grasping later debates on the authority of consensus.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note that this is a quotation of an opponent's view.
The passage begins with 'يتصور' and ends with 'وليس ذلك دليلا في الشرع'.
2
Identify the two parts of the argument
The argument has a rational part (starting with 'أما العقل') and a historical part (starting with 'فانظر كيف'). Find each.
3
Examine the rational proof
Notice how the argument moves from differences in nature to differences in judgment, and uses the rhetorical question 'أليس أحق الأحكام...' to assert that even the most important matter (the caliphate) did not achieve consensus.
The rational proof is based on the variability of ijtihad.
4
Analyze the historical evidence
Look at the events at Saqifa: the Ansar's claim, the agreement on Sa'd ibn Ubada, Umar's quick pledge, and the subsequent disputes. Consider how the passage uses these to claim consensus was absent.
The passage cites Abu Bakr's own words calling it a 'feltah' (spontaneous event).
5
Understand the conclusion
The opponents conclude: if consensus did not occur in the most important matter, it will never occur, so it cannot be a proof. Ask yourself: is this a valid argument?
This argument is presented for refutation; the author likely rejects it.

يتصور يكون باختيار من المجتهدين والاختيار من كل واحد من الأمة إجماعا، بحيث لا يقدر فيه اختلاف لا عقلا ولا وقوعا، أما العقل فإن الاختيار إذا كان مبنيًا على الاجتهاد والاجتهاد ينبني على ما تعين لكل واحد من العقلاء من قضايا تردده في الوجوه العقلية والسمعية، وذلك إذا كان مختلفا في الطباع فبالضرورة أن مختلفا في الحكم، أليس أحق الأحكام بوجود الاتفاق فيه الخلافة الأولى؟ وأولى الأزمان في الشرع هو الزمان الأول؟ وأولى الأشخاص بالصدق والإخلاص الصحابة؟ وأحق الصحابة بالأمانة ونفي التهمة والخيانة المهاجرون والأنصار؟ وأقرب الناس إلى رسول الله أبو بكر وعمر؟ فانظر كيف انحاز الأنصار إلى السقيفة وكيف قالوا: منا أمير ومنكم أمير وكيف أجمعوا على سعد بن عبادة لولا أن تداركه عمر بأن بايع بنفسه حتى شايعه الناس، ثم قال بعد ذلك: ألا إن بيعة أبي بكر كانت فلتة فوقى الله شرها، فمن عاد إلى مثلها فاقتلوه فأيما رجل بايع رجلا من غير مشورة من المسلمين فإنها أن يقتلا؛ يعني أني بايعت أبا بكر وما شاروت الجماعة، ووقى الله شرها فلا تعودوا إلى مثلها، ولم يكن وقت البيعة اتفاق الجماعة وفي الغد لما بايعوه انحازت بنو أمية وبنو هاشم حتى قال أبو سفيان لعلي رضي الله عنه: لم تدع هذا الأمر يكون في شر قبيلة من قريش، فأجابه علي: فتنتنا وأنت كافر، وتريد أن تغره حتى تفتننا وأنت مسلم، وقال العباس قولا مثل ذلك، وقد سمع النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم: أنت أبي وأبو بقية الأمة، الخلافة في ولدك وما اختلف الليل والنهار، ولم يخرج علي رضي الله عنه إلى البيعة حتى قيل: إنه كان له بيعة في السر وبيعة في أسامة العلانية، وقد خرج بن زيد وهو على جيشه أمير بتأمير النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم. قالوا: فإذا لم يتصور إجماع الأمة في أهم الأمور وأولاها بالاعتبار دل على أن الإجماع لن يتحقق قط، وليس ذلك دليلا في الشرع.
It is conceivable that [the appointment] be by the choice of the mujtahids, and the choice of each one of the Ummah is a consensus, such that no disagreement is possible in it, neither rationally nor in occurrence. As for reason, if choice is based on ijtihad, and ijtihad is built upon what is determined for each of the rational beings from the propositions of his hesitation in the rational and scriptural considerations, and if that differs in nature, then necessarily the judgment differs. Is not the most deserving of judgments to have agreement in it the first caliphate? Is not the most deserving of times in the Sharia the first time? Are not the most deserving people of truthfulness and sincerity the Companions? Are not the most deserving of the Companions of trustworthiness and negation of suspicion and treachery the Emigrants and the Helpers? Are not the closest people to the Messenger of God Abu Bakr and Umar? So look how the Helpers gathered at Saqifa and how they said: 'From us a leader and from you a leader,' and how they agreed upon Sa'd ibn Ubada, had it not been that Umar overtook him by pledging allegiance himself until the people followed him. Then he said afterwards: Indeed, the allegiance of Abu Bakr was a spontaneous event, and God protected you from its evil. So whoever returns to something like it, kill him. Any man who pledges allegiance to a man without consultation of the Muslims, they should both be killed. Meaning: I pledged allegiance to Abu Bakr and I did not consult the community, and God protected you from its evil, so do not return to something like it. And at the time of the allegiance there was no agreement of the community, and the next day when they pledged allegiance to him, the Umayyads and Hashimites withdrew, until Abu Sufyan said to Ali, may God be pleased with him: 'Why do you leave this matter to be in the worst tribe of Quraysh?' Ali answered him: 'You caused us trial while you were a disbeliever, and you want to deceive him until you cause us trial while you are a Muslim.' And al-Abbas said a similar saying. And the Prophet, peace be upon him, was heard to say: 'You are my father and the father of the rest of the Ummah; the caliphate is in your progeny as long as night and day alternate.' And Ali did not go out to the allegiance until it was said: He had an allegiance in secret and an allegiance in public as Usama. And [Usama?] ibn Zaid came out while he was the commander of his army by the appointment of the Prophet, peace be upon him. They said: If consensus of the Ummah is not conceivable in the most important matters and the most deserving of consideration, then that indicates that consensus will never be realized, and that is not a proof in the Sharia.
Source pages p. 482

Commentary
This passage is a quotation of an argument made by opponents (indicated by 'they said') against the possibility of consensus (ijma') being a valid proof in religious law. The argument is in two parts: a rational proof and an empirical historical proof. The rational proof begins by stating that consensus, if it exists, would require all mujtahids to agree on the same ruling. But ijtihad is based on each individual's reasoning from rational and scriptural premises, and since people's natures differ, their judgments necessarily differ. Therefore, agreement on the most important matters—like the caliphate—is impossible. The historical proof then provides examples from the election of Abu Bakr at Saqifa: the Ansar wanted a leader from among themselves, they agreed on Sa'd ibn Ubada, but Umar's quick pledge to Abu Bakr averted that; however, there was no prior consultation, and later Abu Bakr himself called it a 'spontaneous event' (feltah) that God protected them from repeating. The passage recounts that Banu Umayya and Banu Hashim withdrew, Abu Sufyan and al-Abbas objected, and Ali initially withheld his allegiance. From these events, the opponents conclude that consensus did not occur even in the most important matter (the caliphate), so consensus is impossible to achieve and cannot be a proof in Sharia. The author is likely presenting this view to refute it later.

Key Terms
إجماع
اجتهاد
خلفاء
سقيفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument claims consensus is invalid per se.
Correction: The argument does not attack the validity of consensus as a source of law; rather, it argues that consensus is practically impossible to achieve, and therefore cannot be a reliable proof.

Checkpoint
According to the argument presented, why is consensus impossible?
A) Because the Quran prohibits it
B) Because differences in ijtihad and nature lead to different judgments
C) Because the companions never agreed on anything
D) Because the Prophet forbade consensus

Key Takeaway
This argument challenges the possibility of consensus based on natural differences in reasoning and a historical case study, but the author presents it to refute it later.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Are you quick to condemn those who differ with you on subsidiary issues? Ask Allah to give you humility and understanding of the nature of ijtihad.
?
The opponent in this passage used the lack of consensus at Saqifa to reject the principle of ijma entirely. Reflect: Are there principles in your faith or life that you have abandoned because of one failure or disagreement? How does this warning about misusing a single event apply to your own reasoning? Commit to not letting a single case lead you to discard a truth.
