The Logical Contradiction in Appointing an Imam by Choice
Opponents argue that appointment by choice leads to two contradictions, proving imamate is not obligatory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two logical contradictions that opponents use to argue that the imamate cannot be obligatory if appointed by choice.

Core Idea
If the electors make the imam and then must obey him, his authority is self-referentially contradictory.

The opponents of obligatory imamate present a clever logical problem: if the community chooses the imam, how can he become obligated to obey? This passage lays out two contradictions that challenge the consistency of making imamate a religious duty. Let's follow their reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to get the overall argument. Notice the two aspects it mentions.
The passage ends mid-sentence; the author will likely refute this later.
2
Identify the first contradiction
The elector who appoints the imam becomes obligated to obey him. But the imam only becomes imam by that appointment. So the same act creates authority and imposes obedience—a circular problem.
3
Identify the second contradiction
Each elector is a mujtahid who can validly oppose the imam on ijtihadi matters. If the imam's rulings can be legitimately opposed, how can his obedience be mandatory?
This undermines the absolute authority of the imam.
4
Understand the conclusion
From these contradictions, the opponents conclude that imamate is not a religious obligation. It is only permissible if needed, provided the leader is just.
The passage ends with 'until', possibly listing further conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
:قالوا ونصب الإمامة بالاختيار متناقض من وجهين؛ أحدهما أن صاحب الاختيار موجب في النصب على الإمام حتى يصير إمامًا، ويجب عليه طاعته إذا قام بالإمامة، فهو إنما صار إمامًا بإقامته فكيف صار واجب الطاعة بإمامته، والثاني أن كل واحد من المجتهدين الناصبين للإمامة إن خالف الإمام في المسائل الاجتهادية باجتهاده جاز له ذلك، وما من مسألة فرضتم وجوب الطاعة لله فيها إلا ويجوز له فيها باجتهاده فكيف نجعله إمامًا واجب الطاعة بشرط أن يخالفه إذا أدى إلى المخالفة اجتهاده. :قالوا فدل هذا كله على أن الإمامة غير واجبة في الشرع، نعم لو احتاجوا إلى رئيس يحمي بيضة الإسلام ويجمع شمل الأنام، وأدى اجتهادهم إلى نصبه مقدما عليهم جاز ذلك؛ بشرط أن يبقى في معاملاته على النصفة والعدل، حتى
: They said: And the appointment of the imam by choice is contradictory in two aspects: One of them is that the one who chooses is the one who effects the appointment of the imam until he becomes an imam, and it becomes obligatory to obey him when he undertakes the imamate. So he only became an imam by his own undertaking? How then does he become obligatory of obedience by his imamate? The second is that every one of the mujtahids who appoint the imam, if he opposes the imam in ijtihadi matters by his own ijtihad, that is permissible for him. And there is no matter in which you have assumed the obligation of obedience to God except that it is permissible for him to [differ] by ijtihad. So how can we make him an imam whose obedience is obligatory on the condition that he be opposed if his ijtihad leads to opposition. : They said: All of this indicates that the imamate is not obligatory in the Sharia. Yes, if they need a leader to protect the Islamic domain and unite the people, and their ijtihad leads to his appointment as their leader, that is permissible, on the condition that he remains in his dealings on equity and justice, until
Source pages p. 482

Commentary
This passage continues the opponents' argument against the necessity of imamate (the institution of a leader). It presents a specific logical objection to the theory that the imam is appointed by choice (ikhtiyar). The objection has two aspects: First, the person or group making the choice (the electors) are the ones who cause the imam to become imam, yet they then must obey him. This is contradictory because the same action that creates authority also imposes obedience—the imam's authority is self-referential (he becomes imam by their choice, then they must obey him). Second, each mujtahid elector, if he disagrees with the imam on a matter of ijtihad, is allowed to follow his own ijtihad, and there is no matter in which obedience to God is mandatory except that the mujtahid may validly differ. So how can we make the imam an obligatory authority when his rulings can be legitimately opposed by those who appointed him? The opponents conclude from these contradictions that imamate is not an obligation in Sharia; it is merely a permissible arrangement if the community needs a leader for protection and unity, provided he acts justly. The passage ends mid-sentence with 'until', possibly continuing to state conditions. The author is likely presenting this for refutation.

Key Terms
إمامة
اختيار
اجتهاد
طاعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument denies the validity or importance of having an imam altogether.
Correction: The argument only attacks the logical consistency of making imamate obligatory through choice; it does not negate the practical need for a leader.

Checkpoint
What is the second aspect of the contradiction in appointing an imam by choice?
A) The imam cannot rule justly
B) The electors may validly oppose the imam on ijtihadi matters, so his obedience cannot be absolute
C) The imam must be from Quraysh
D) The appointment requires consensus

Key Takeaway
Opponents of obligatory imamate argue that appointment by choice leads to contradictions that undermine its necessity in Sharia.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a leader whose authority was established by human choice (e.g., an elected official or a community-appointed scholar), do you ever find yourself resenting their authority because you helped put them there? How does this lesson's first contradiction expose a tension in your own heart about submission to those you elevated?
?
Think of a time when your own reasoning (your 'ijtihad') led you to disagree with a religious authority you are supposed to obey. Did you feel justified in dismissing his ruling entirely? This lesson warns against making obedience conditional on your own agreement. Where do you draw the line between legitimate ijtihadi difference and outright rebellion of the heart?
?
The opponents in this passage conclude that imamate is not a religious obligation, only a practical arrangement. If you truly believed that no human authority was absolutely binding, would that change how you relate to your leaders—in the mosque, in your home, or in society? What would you do differently starting tomorrow?

Opposition Arguments Against Obligatory Imamate
Opposition argues imamate is not obligatory and permits it conditionally.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the main arguments used by opponents to deny the obligatory nature of imamate, including historical challenges to consensus and logical contradictions in appointment by choice.

Core Idea
Opponents argue imamate is not obligatory, but conditionally permissible for practical needs.

This passage presents the case of those who reject the obligation of imamate. They begin by citing historical difficulties in achieving consensus on the caliphate, then highlight two logical problems with the method of appointment by choice. Ultimately, they concede that a leader may be appointed conditionally for social order, but with the right of deposition if unjust.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it, then read the translation. Let the author's flow come through before you interpret.
The opening lines may be unclear due to OCR; focus on the main argument.
2
Identify the historical evidence
Notice how the opponents use historical incidents to argue that consensus on the imamate was never achieved, implying that it cannot be a proof for obligation.
They mention events around Ali, al-Abbas, and others.
3
Analyze the two contradictions
The opponents present two logical contradictions in the appointment by choice: (1) circular causation, (2) conflict with ijtihad. Understand each one.
The second contradiction is about the imam being both obligatory to obey yet permissible to disagree with in ijtihad.
4
Understand the conditional acceptance
See that despite denying obligation, they allow a conditional leader for protection and unity, but with strict conditions of justice and the right to depose.
They cite Uthman and Ali as examples of deposition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تفتننا وأنت مسلم، وقال العباس قولاً مثل ذلك، وقد سمع النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم: أنت أبي وأبو بقية الأمة، الخلافة في ولدك وما اختلف الليل والنهار، ولم يخرج علي رضي الله عنه إلى البيعة حتى قيل: إنه كان له بيعة في السر وبيعة في أسامة العلانية، وقد خرج بن زيد وهو على جيشه أمير بتأمير النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم. قالوا: فإذا لم يتصور إجماع الأمة في أهم الأمور وأولاها بالاعتبار دل على أن الإجماع لن يتحقق قط، وليس ذلك دليلاً في الشرع. قالوا: ونصب الإمامة بالاختيار متناقض من وجهين؛ أحدهما أن صاحب الاختيار موجب في النصب على الإمام حتى يصير إماماً، ويجب عليه طاعته إذا قام بالإمامة، فهو إنما صار إماماً بإقامته فكيف صار واجب الطاعة بإمامته، والثاني أن كل واحد من المجتهدين الناصبين للإمامة إذا خالف الإمام في المسائل الاجتهادية باجتهاده جاز له ذلك، وما من مسألة فرضتم وجوب الطاعة لله فيها إلا ويجوز له فيها باجتهاده فكيف نجعله إماماً واجب الطاعة بشرط أن يخالفه إذا أدى اجتهاده إلى المخالفة. قالوا: فدل هذا كله على أن الإمامة غير واجبة في الشرع، نعم لو احتاجوا إلى رئيس يحمي بيضة الإسلام ويجمع شمل الأنام، وأدى اجتهادهم إلى نصبه مقدماً عليهم جاز ذلك؛ بشرط أن يبقى في معاملاته على النصفة والعدل، حتى إذا جار في قضية على واحد وجب عليهم خلعه ومنابذته، وهذا كما فعلوا بعثمان وعلي رضي الله عنهما، فإنه لما أحدث عثمان تلك الأحداث خلعوه فلما لم ينخلع قتلوه، ولما رضي علي بالتحكيم وشك في إمامته خلعوه وقاتلوه.
You test us while you are Muslim, and al-Abbas said a similar saying, and the Prophet (peace be upon him) heard: 'You are my father and the father of the rest of the community, the caliphate in your son as long as night and day alternate,' and Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) did not come out for the oath of allegiance until it was said: he had a secret oath of allegiance and a public oath of allegiance in Usama's affair, and Ibn Zayd went out while he was a commander of his army by the appointment of the Prophet (peace be upon him). They said: If consensus of the community cannot be conceived in the most important matters and the most deserving of consideration, it indicates that consensus will never be realized, and that is not a proof in the Law. They said: And the appointment of the imamate by choice is contradictory in two respects: one is that the one with choice is the cause in the appointment of the imam until he becomes imam, and obedience to him becomes obligatory when he assumes the imamate, so he only became imam by his assumption, so how does he become obligatory of obedience by his imamate? The second is that every one of the mujtahids who appoint the imam, if he disagrees with the imam in jurisprudential matters according to his ijtihad, that is permissible for him, and there is no matter in which you suppose obedience to Allah is obligatory except that ijtihad is permitted for him in it, so how do we make him an imam obligatory of obedience on condition that he disagree with him if his ijtihad leads to opposition? They said: All this indicates that the imamate is not obligatory in the Law. Yes, if they need a leader to protect the sanctuary of Islam and unite the people, and their ijtihad leads to appointing him as a leader over them, that is permissible; on condition that he remain in his dealings in fairness and justice, until if he is unjust in a case against someone, they must depose him and oppose him. This is what they did with Uthman and Ali (may Allah be pleased with them): when Uthman brought about those events, they deposed him, and when he did not step down, they killed him; and when Ali agreed to arbitration and there was doubt about his imamate, they deposed him and fought him.
Source pages p. 483

Commentary
This passage presents the arguments of those who deny that the imamate is obligatory in Islamic law (shari'a). The author begins by citing historical incidents to show that consensus among the companions was not achieved even on crucial matters like the caliphate, implying that consensus (ijma') is unattainable and therefore cannot serve as a proof for obligation. Then the opposition presents two logical contradictions in the idea of appointing an imam by choice (ikhtiyar): first, the one making the choice causes the imam to become imam, but then obedience becomes obligatory due to his imamate, creating a circular dependence; second, each mujtahid who appoints the imam has the right to disagree with him in ijtihad matters, so how can the imam be deserving of unconditional obedience if disagreement is permitted? The conclusion is that imamate is not legally obligatory. However, they concede that if people need a leader for practical purposes, they may appoint one conditionally, provided he acts justly; if he becomes unjust, they must depose him. They cite the examples of Uthman and Ali to illustrate this conditional appointment and deposition.

Key Terms
إجماع
إمامة
اختيار
نصب
طاعة
خلع
اجتهاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that denying obligatory imamate means rejecting any leadership or advocating anarchy.
Correction: The opponents allow a conditional leader for protection and unity, but not as a religious obligation.

Checkpoint
What do the opponents claim about the possibility of consensus (ijma') regarding the imamate?
A) It is easily attainable.
B) It is impossible to achieve.
C) It is required for the imamate to be valid.
D) It is only possible in non-essential matters.

Key Takeaway
Opponents of obligatory imamate rely on historical lack of consensus, logical contradictions in appointment, and permit only conditional leadership subject to justice and deposition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How do you respond when the leaders you trust act unjustly? Do you have the courage to hold them accountable, as the early Muslims did with Uthman and Ali, or do you remain silent out of convenience? What would it cost you to speak up?
?
The opponents argued that obeying an imam is contradictory because ijtihad may lead to disagreement. In your own life, when does your obedience to authority conflict with your personal convictions? How do you resolve that tension, and what would the ummah look like if everyone resolved it the way you do?
?
If you were part of a community that needed a leader to 'protect the sanctuary of Islam and unite the people,' would you be willing to appoint a leader on the condition that you could depose them if they became unjust? What does that say about your trust in leadership—and in your own ability to judge when justice has been betrayed?

Shi'a Proof for Obligatory Imamate by Reason and Revelation
The Shi'a argue imamate is obligatory by reason and scripture, likening it to prophethood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Shi'a justify the necessity of the imamate using both rational analogy and scriptural commands.

Core Idea
The imamate is as necessary as prophethood — reason and revelation both require it.

The Shi'a position on the imamate is grounded in both intellectual necessity and divine command. In this passage, the author presents their case by drawing an analogy to the need for prophets and by citing Quranic verses commanding obedience to authority and truthfulness. This lesson unpacks that dual argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic text and its literal translation to grasp the overall argument before analysis.
Note the concise style and technical terms like 'lutf' and 'hikma'.
2
Identify the rational analogy
Look for the comparison between the need for an imam and the need for a prophet. Understand why the Shi'a say the preservation of shari'a is as important as its establishment.
The argument uses the concept of 'lutf' (grace) or 'hikma' (wisdom) as a basis.
3
Examine the scriptural evidence
Focus on the two Quranic verses cited: 4:59 (obey those in authority) and 9:119 (be with the truthful). Consider how these imply the existence of an infallible imam.
The author argues that without an imam, these commands would be impossible to fulfill.
4
Trace the conclusion on infallibility
Notice how the passage defines 'isma' (infallibility) as truthfulness in all statements, linking it to the necessity of an absolutely truthful person in the community.
This connects the scriptural command to the theological doctrine of the imam's sinlessness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالت الشيعة الإمامة واجبة في الدين عقلاً وشرعاً، كما أن النبوة واجبة في الفطرة عقلاً وسمعاً. أما وجوب الإمامة عقلاً أن احتياج الناس إلى إمام واجب الطاعة يحفظ أحكام الشرع عليهم ويحملهم على مراعاة حدود الدين كاحتياج الناس إلى نبي مرسل يشرع لهم الأحكام ويبين لهم الحلال والحرام، واحتياج الخلق إلى استبقاء الشرع كاحتياجهم إلى تمهيد الشرع، وإذا كان الأول واجباً إما لطفاً من الله تعالى وإما حكمة عقلية واجبة كان الثاني واجباً. وأما السمع فإن الله تعالى أمرنا بمتابعة أولي الأمر وطاعتهم فقال: (أطيعوا الله والرسول وأولي الأمر منكم) [النساء: 59]، فإذا لم يكن إمام واجب الطاعة كيف يلزمنا ذلك التكليف؟ وقال الله تعالى: (يا أيها الذين آمنوا اتقوا الله وكونوا مع الصادقين) [التوبة: 119]، فلو لم يكن في الأمة صادقون واجبو الطاعة كيف وجب علينا أن نكون معهم، ويستحيل أن يكلف إنسان كن مع فلان ولا فلان في العالم، وإذا كان العالم لا يخلو عن صادق مطلق فقد تحققت عصمته، فإنا لا نعني بالعصمة إلا الصدق في جميع الأقوال، ومن كان صادقاً في جميع الأقوال كان صالحاً في جميع الأحوال.
The Shi'a said: The imamate is obligatory in religion by reason and revelation, just as prophethood is obligatory in the natural disposition by reason and audition. As for the obligation of imamate by reason: the people's need for an imam whose obedience is obligatory, who preserves the rulings of shari'a upon them and compels them to observe the limits of the religion, is like the people's need for a sent prophet who legislates for them the rulings and explains to them the lawful and the unlawful. And the creation's need for the preservation of the shari'a is like their need for the establishment of the shari'a. If the first is obligatory, either as a grace from Allah or as a necessary rational wisdom, then the second is obligatory. As for audition (the revealed evidence): Allah Almighty commanded us to follow the holders of authority and obey them, saying: 'Obey Allah and the Messenger and those in authority among you' [4:59]. If there is no imam whose obedience is obligatory, how can that obligation be binding upon us? And Allah Almighty said: 'O you who have believed, fear Allah and be with the truthful' [9:119]. If there were no truthful ones among the community whose obedience is obligatory, how would we be obligated to be with them? And it is impossible for a person to be charged with 'be with so-and-so and not with so-and-so' in the world. And if the world cannot be devoid of an absolutely truthful person, then his infallibility is realized; for we do not mean by infallibility except truthfulness in all statements, and whoever is truthful in all statements is righteous in all states.
Source pages p. 484

Commentary
This passage presents the Shi'a argument for the necessity of the imamate, claiming it is obligatory by both reason (aql) and revelation (sam'). The rational proof is an analogy: just as people need a prophet to establish the shari'a initially, they need an imam to preserve it. Since the need is similar, the obligation is similar. The obligation may be understood as a grace (lutf) from Allah or as a rational necessity. The scriptural proof uses two Quranic verses: 4:59 commands obedience to 'those in authority' (uli al-amr), implying the existence of an imam whose obedience is obligatory; otherwise the command would be impossible. Similarly, 9:119 commands being with 'the truthful' (al-sadiqin), implying the existence of infallible, truthful individuals in the community. The passage then defines infallibility (isma) as truthfulness in all statements, leading to righteousness in all actions. Thus, the imam must be infallible.

Key Terms
إمامة
عقل
سمع
لطف
حكمة
أولي الأمر
صادقين
عصمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Shi'a argue for imamate only from revelation.
Correction: The passage shows they also provide a rational analogy to prophethood, grounding imamate in both reason and revelation.

Checkpoint
According to the Shi'a rational argument, why is the imamate obligatory?
A) Because it is mentioned in the Quran.
B) Because the need for preserving the shari'a is like the need for establishing it.
C) Because the imam must be a descendant of the Prophet.
D) Because consensus of the community requires it.

Key Takeaway
The Shi'a defend the imamate as a rational and scriptural necessity, comparing it to prophethood and deriving it from Quranic commands to obey authority and be with the truthful.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Shi'a argue that an imam is needed to preserve the shari'a, just as a prophet was needed to establish it. Think about who you trust to preserve and explain the religion to you today. Are you absolutely certain that your teachers or scholars are truthful in all matters? If not, how do you ensure you are not following error? This lesson forces you to confront whether you have a reliable guardian of faith, or if you are left to your own devices.
?
The passage defines infallibility as complete truthfulness in speech, leading to righteousness in action. Examine your own life: can you honestly say that every statement you make is truthful? What does your answer reveal about your own righteousness? Are you willing to commit to absolute truthfulness, knowing that it is the foundation of being among the truthful that Allah commands you to be with?
?
The Shi'a use both reason and scripture to prove imamate. Consider your own belief system: do you have rational arguments for why you follow the particular leaders or scholars you do? Or are you simply following tradition? This lesson demands that you ask yourself: is your faith based on a reasoned conviction, or on blind acceptance? What would it take for you to be able to defend your beliefs with both reason and scripture as the Shi'a do for theirs?

Historical Precedent for Deposing Unjust Rulers
Historical examples of deposing unjust rulers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the historical precedent used by the author to justify deposing unjust rulers, specifically the examples of Uthman and Ali.

Core Idea
Deposing an unjust ruler was a practice of the early Muslim community.

The author begins by stating a principle: when a ruler becomes unjust, the community must depose him. He then cites the historical examples of Uthman and Ali to illustrate this principle. This sets the stage for the subsequent discussion on the imamate.

Guided Path
1
Read the principle and examples
Read the passage that states the rule and gives the examples of Uthman and Ali. Notice how the author uses these events as evidence.
These examples are historically controversial.
2
Consider the implications
Think about how this historical precedent relates to the question of the imamate. Does it support or challenge later arguments?
The author is setting up a framework for what may be done to a ruler.
3
Reflect on the method
Observe that the author uses historical practice (sunnah of the companions) as a proof. This is a common theological method.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذا جار في قضية على واحد وجب عليهم خلعه ومنابذته، وهذا كما فعلوا بعثمان وعلي رضي الله عنهما، فإنه لما أحدث عثمان تلك الأحداث خلعوه فلما لم ينخلع قتلوه، ولما رضي علي بالتحكيم وشك في إمامته خلعوه وقاتلوه. قالت الشيعة الإمامة واجبة في الدين عقلا وشرعا، كما أن النبوة واجبة في الفطرة عقلا وسمعاً. أما وجوب الإمامة عقلاً أن احتياج الناس إلى إمام واجب الطاعة يحفظ أحكام الشرع عليهم ويحملهم على مراعاة حدود الدين كاحتياج الناس إلى نبي مرسل يشرع لهم الأحكام ويبين لهم الحلال والحرام، واحتياج الخلق إلى استبقاء الشرع كاحتياجهم إلى تمهيد الشرع، وإذا كان الأول واجبًا إما لطفا من الله تعالى وإما حكمة عقلية واجبة كان الثاني واجبًا. وأما السمع فإن الله تعالى أمرنا بمتابعة أولي الأمر وطاعتهم فقال: (أطيعوا الله والرسول وأولي الأمر منكم) [النساء: 59]، فإذا لم يكن إمام واجب الطاعة كيف يلزمنا ذلك التكليف؟ وقال الله تعالى: (يا أيها الذين آمنوا اتقوا الله وكونوا مع الصادقين) [التوبة: 119] فلو لم يكن في الأمة صادقون واجبو الطاعة كيف وجب علينا أن نكون معهم، ويستحيل أن يكلف إنسان كن مع فلان ولا فلان في العالم، وإذا كان العالم لا يخلو عن صادق مطلق فقد تحققت عصمته، فإنا لا نعني بالعصمة إلا الصدق في جميع الأقوال، ومن كان صادقًا في جميع الأقوال كان صالحا في جميع الأحوال.
If one is unjust in a matter, it is obligatory for them to depose him and oppose him. This is as they did with Uthman and Ali (may Allah be pleased with them). For when Uthman committed those innovations, they deposed him, and when he did not step down, they killed him. And when Ali acquiesced to arbitration and doubt was cast on his imamate, they deposed him and fought him. The Shi‘a said: The imamate is obligatory in religion both rationally and by revelation, just as prophethood is obligatory in the natural constitution rationally and by tradition. As for the rational obligation of the imamate: The need of people for an imam whose obedience is obligatory, who preserves for them the rulings of the law and compels them to observe the boundaries of religion, is like the need of people for a sent prophet who legislates for them the rulings and makes clear to them the lawful and the unlawful. And the need of creatures for the preservation of the law is like their need for the establishment of the law. If the first is obligatory, either as a grace from Allah or as a necessary rational wisdom, then the second is obligatory. As for the scriptural [proof]: Allah commanded us to follow those in authority and obey them, saying: 'Obey Allah and obey the Messenger and those in authority among you' (Qur'an 4:59). So if there is no imam whose obedience is obligatory, how does that obligation bind us? And Allah said: 'O you who believe, fear Allah and be with the truthful' (Qur'an 9:119). So if there were no truthful ones in the community whose obedience is obligatory, how would it be obligatory for us to be with them? It is impossible that a person be commanded: 'Be with so-and-so' but no such person exists in the world. And if the world never lacks a truthful person absolutely, his infallibility is thereby realized. For we mean by infallibility nothing but truthfulness in all statements, and whoever is truthful in all statements is upright in all circumstances.
Source pages p. 484

Commentary
This passage presents two distinct elements: first, a historical precedent about the companions deposing unjust rulers, and second, the Shi‘a argument for the obligation of the imamate. The author begins by stating a principle: if a ruler becomes unjust, the community must depose him and oppose him. He gives the examples of Uthman and Ali: Uthman was deposed and killed when he persisted in innovations, and Ali was fought when he accepted arbitration, which cast doubt on his imamate. This sets the stage for the Shi‘a position. Then the Shi‘a are quoted as saying that the imamate is obligatory both rationally and by revelation, just as prophethood is obligatory by nature (fitra) rationally and traditionally. They then provide two proofs. First, the rational proof: people need an imam who preserves the law and compels obedience, just as they need a prophet to establish the law. If the first (prophethood) is obligatory either as a divine grace (lutf) or as a rational necessity, then the second (imamate) is also obligatory, because the need for preservation is analogous to the need for establishment. Second, the scriptural proof: the Qur'an commands obedience to 'those in authority' (uli al-amr) and commands being with 'the truthful' (al-sadiqin). For these commands to be meaningful, there must exist an imam whose obedience is obligatory and a truthful person (i.e., an infallible imam) to whose company we are commanded. The world cannot be without such a truthful person; otherwise the command would be impossible. This truthful person is infallible, because infallibility here means truthfulness in all statements, which implies uprightness in all actions. Thus, the existence of an infallible imam is necessary from the Qur'anic commands.

Key Terms

العصمة
اللطف
الحكمة العقلية
أولو الأمر

Checkpoint

B) He committed innovations.
C) He was unjust in a legal case.
D) He refused to pay zakat.

Key Takeaway
The historical examples of Uthman and Ali show that the early community acted on the principle that an unjust ruler must be opposed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you were part of the early community, would you have participated in deposing Uthman or Ali? What internal conflict would you have faced between loyalty and justice?
?
When you see injustice from a leader you respect, what stops you from speaking out? Are you more afraid of the leader or of Allah's command to enjoin justice?
?
This passage shows that even rightly guided caliphs were opposed when they did wrong. Is there any ruler or authority figure in your life whose injustice you have excused? What would it cost you to oppose them? Will you pay that price?

Shi'a Rational and Scriptural Proofs for the Imamate
Shi'a rational and scriptural proofs for imamate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Shi'a arguments for the obligatoriness of the imamate, both from reason and from scripture.

Core Idea
The imamate is as necessary as prophethood, both rationally and scripturally.

The Shi'a argue that the imamate is obligatory in religion, just as prophethood is. They provide a rational analogy: the need for an imam to preserve the law is like the need for a prophet to establish it. They also cite verses commanding obedience to those in authority and companionship with the truthful, arguing that such commands imply the existence of an infallible imam.

Guided Path
1
Understand the claim
The Shi'a assert that the imamate is obligatory both rationally and by revelation, similar to prophethood.
They base this on the need for guidance after the Prophet.
2
Follow the rational analogy
The rational proof compares the need for an imam to preserve the law to the need for a prophet to establish it. If prophethood is obligatory, so is imamate.
The argument relies on the principles of divine grace (lutf) and rational wisdom.
3
Examine the scriptural proof from Qur'an
Verse 4:59 commands obedience to 'those in authority' (uli al-amr). For this command to be meaningful, there must be a specific imam whose obedience is obligatory. Similarly, verse 9:119 commands being with 'the truthful' (al-sadiqin), implying an infallible imam exists in every age.
Infallibility is defined as truthfulness in all statements.
4
See the conclusion
Since the world cannot be without a truthful person, an infallible imam must always exist. This imam is necessary for the validity of divine commands.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذا جار في قضية على واحد وجب عليهم خلعه ومنابذته، وهذا كما فعلوا بعثمان وعلي رضي الله عنهما، فإنه لما أحدث عثمان تلك الأحداث خلعوه فلما لم ينخلع قتلوه، ولما رضي علي بالتحكيم وشك في إمامته خلعوه وقاتلوه. قالت الشيعة الإمامة واجبة في الدين عقلا وشرعا، كما أن النبوة واجبة في الفطرة عقلا وسمعاً. أما وجوب الإمامة عقلاً أن احتياج الناس إلى إمام واجب الطاعة يحفظ أحكام الشرع عليهم ويحملهم على مراعاة حدود الدين كاحتياج الناس إلى نبي مرسل يشرع لهم الأحكام ويبين لهم الحلال والحرام، واحتياج الخلق إلى استبقاء الشرع كاحتياجهم إلى تمهيد الشرع، وإذا كان الأول واجبًا إما لطفا من الله تعالى وإما حكمة عقلية واجبة كان الثاني واجبًا. وأما السمع فإن الله تعالى أمرنا بمتابعة أولي الأمر وطاعتهم فقال: (أطيعوا الله والرسول وأولي الأمر منكم) [النساء: 59]، فإذا لم يكن إمام واجب الطاعة كيف يلزمنا ذلك التكليف؟ وقال الله تعالى: (يا أيها الذين آمنوا اتقوا الله وكونوا مع الصادقين) [التوبة: 119] فلو لم يكن في الأمة صادقون واجبو الطاعة كيف وجب علينا أن نكون معهم، ويستحيل أن يكلف إنسان كن مع فلان ولا فلان في العالم، وإذا كان العالم لا يخلو عن صادق مطلق فقد تحققت عصمته، فإنا لا نعني بالعصمة إلا الصدق في جميع الأقوال، ومن كان صادقًا في جميع الأقوال كان صالحا في جميع الأحوال.
If one is unjust in a matter, it is obligatory for them to depose him and oppose him. This is as they did with Uthman and Ali (may Allah be pleased with them). For when Uthman committed those innovations, they deposed him, and when he did not step down, they killed him. And when Ali acquiesced to arbitration and doubt was cast on his imamate, they deposed him and fought him. The Shi‘a said: The imamate is obligatory in religion both rationally and by revelation, just as prophethood is obligatory in the natural constitution rationally and by tradition. As for the rational obligation of the imamate: The need of people for an imam whose obedience is obligatory, who preserves for them the rulings of the law and compels them to observe the boundaries of religion, is like the need of people for a sent prophet who legislates for them the rulings and makes clear to them the lawful and the unlawful. And the need of creatures for the preservation of the law is like their need for the establishment of the law. If the first is obligatory, either as a grace from Allah or as a necessary rational wisdom, then the second is obligatory. As for the scriptural [proof]: Allah commanded us to follow those in authority and obey them, saying: 'Obey Allah and obey the Messenger and those in authority among you' (Qur'an 4:59). So if there is no imam whose obedience is obligatory, how does that obligation bind us? And Allah said: 'O you who believe, fear Allah and be with the truthful' (Qur'an 9:119). So if there were no truthful ones in the community whose obedience is obligatory, how would it be obligatory for us to be with them? It is impossible that a person be commanded: 'Be with so-and-so' but no such person exists in the world. And if the world never lacks a truthful person absolutely, his infallibility is thereby realized. For we mean by infallibility nothing but truthfulness in all statements, and whoever is truthful in all statements is upright in all circumstances.
Source pages p. 484

Commentary
This passage presents two distinct elements: first, a historical precedent about the companions deposing unjust rulers, and second, the Shi‘a argument for the obligation of the imamate. The author begins by stating a principle: if a ruler becomes unjust, the community must depose him and oppose him. He gives the examples of Uthman and Ali: Uthman was deposed and killed when he persisted in innovations, and Ali was fought when he accepted arbitration, which cast doubt on his imamate. This sets the stage for the Shi‘a position. Then the Shi‘a are quoted as saying that the imamate is obligatory both rationally and by revelation, just as prophethood is obligatory by nature (fitra) rationally and traditionally. They then provide two proofs. First, the rational proof: people need an imam who preserves the law and compels obedience, just as they need a prophet to establish the law. If the first (prophethood) is obligatory either as a divine grace (lutf) or as a rational necessity, then the second (imamate) is also obligatory, because the need for preservation is analogous to the need for establishment. Second, the scriptural proof: the Qur'an commands obedience to 'those in authority' (uli al-amr) and commands being with 'the truthful' (al-sadiqin). For these commands to be meaningful, there must exist an imam whose obedience is obligatory and a truthful person (i.e., an infallible imam) to whose company we are commanded. The world cannot be without such a truthful person; otherwise the command would be impossible. This truthful person is infallible, because infallibility here means truthfulness in all statements, which implies uprightness in all actions. Thus, the existence of an infallible imam is necessary from the Qur'anic commands.

Key Terms
الإمامة
العصمة
اللطف
الحكمة العقلية
أولو الأمر
الصادقون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The imamate is only a political position and not obligatory in religion.
Correction: The Shi'a argue it is obligatory both rationally and scripturally, as necessary for preserving the law like prophethood.

Checkpoint
According to the rational proof, why is the imamate obligatory?
A) Because the Qur'an commands obedience to those in authority.
B) Because the need to preserve the law is like the need to establish it, and prophethood is obligatory.
C) Because the companions deposed Uthman and Ali.
D) Because the imam must be infallible.

Key Takeaway
The Shi'a argue that the imamate is rationally and scripturally obligatory, and that the existence of an infallible imam is necessary for the validity of divine commands.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Shi‘a define infallibility as truthfulness in all statements. Think about your own speech today: how many statements have you made that were not entirely truthful? What would it cost you to be completely truthful in every word?
?
The passage argues that believers are commanded to 'be with the truthful' (Quran 9:119). Who are the truthful people in your life? Are you actively seeking their company, or do you avoid it because their truthfulness might expose your own compromises?
?
If you truly believed that an infallible guide exists today and must be obeyed, how would that change the way you make decisions? What would you be unwilling to give up?

The Second Shi'a Proof: The Prophet's Duty to Designate an Imam
Shi'a argument that the Prophet must have designated an imam by analogy to minor rulings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Shi'a argument that the Prophet must have designated an imam because he provided detailed rulings for minor issues yet could not have omitted the most important need.

Core Idea
If the Prophet taught how to wipe socks, he must have appointed a leader.

This passage presents the second major Shi'a proof for the necessity of an imam. It argues from the principle of good opinion of the Prophet: since he knew the community's deep need for a leader and yet gave detailed rulings for minor matters, it is inconceivable that he would leave the most important matter without explicit designation. The argument builds an analogy from the Companions' presumed fidelity to revealed texts.

Guided Path
1
Understand the principle of good opinion
The Shi'a begin by stating that we must think well of the Companions, assuming they would never abandon an explicit text for personal choice. This sets the foundation for the same reasoning applied to the Prophet.
2
Identify the Prophet's knowledge of needs
List the needs the Prophet allegedly knew: uniting the community, resolving disputes, enforcing the law, doing justice. These are far greater than minor ritual issues.
The passage mentions 'calling opponents by tongue and sword' as part of the imam's role.
3
Note the contrast with minor rulings
The Prophet gave rulings for matters like istinja, wiping over socks, and tayammum. If he was meticulous for these, how could he omit the imamate?
This is the core of the analogy.
4
See the consequence of omission
Without an imam, the community falls into disagreement with no judge or guide. The argument presents a chaotic scenario.
5
Consider the eschatological complaint
The argument concludes by equating the need for an imam with the need for a messenger, implying that the community could complain to God if no imam was designated.
The passage cuts off but the implication is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقروا ذلك من وجه آخر وقالوا كما يجب حسن الظن بالصحابة أنهم لا يعدلون عن نص ظاهر إلى الاختيار، وهم الصفوة الأولى على طراوة القبول، يجب حسن الظن أيضًا بالرسول أنه إذا علم احتياج الخلق إلى من يجمع شملهم ويرفع الخلاف بينهم ويحملهم على مناهج الشرع وينصف المظلوم وينتصف من الظالم، وأنهم إلى من ينتهج مناهجه في دعوة المخالفين باللسان والسيف أحوج منهم إلى مسائل الاستنجاء والمسح على الخفين والتيمم بالتراب وغير ذلك، فإذا لم يقصر في إيراد حكم في كل باب يستدل به على نظائره من ذلك الباب كيف أمسك عن أهم الأبواب كل الإمساك فلم ينطق به نصا ولا أشار إلى شخص تعيينا ولا ذكره بوصف حتى بقيت الأمة على اختلاف في الأصول والفروع؛ فمن ضال ومن هاد يدعي كل واحد أنه على الحق وخصمه على الباطل، ولا حاكم بينهما. ومن جاهل ومن عالم يدعي كل واحد منهما أنه العالم وخصمه الجاهل ولا هادي لهما، فلئن كان يتوجه للعباد أن يقولوا: (ربنا لولا أرسلت إلينا رسولًا) [القصص: 47/طه: 134] أنه لا يتوجه على الله سؤال أفلا يتوجه للأمة أن يقولوا: ربنا هلا عينت لنا إمامًا نتبع قوله من قبل أن نذل ونخزى، وأن الله تعالى
And they established that from another angle and said: Just as it is obligatory to think well of the Companions that they do not deviate from an explicit text to choice, and they are the first elite upon the freshness of acceptance, it is also obligatory to think well of the Messenger that, when he knew the need of creatures for someone who gathers their dispersed, removes conflict among them, compels them to the paths of the law, does justice to the oppressed, and exacts justice from the oppressor, and that they are more in need of someone who follows his path in calling the opponents by tongue and sword than they are of matters of cleansing after relieving oneself, wiping over socks, dry ablution with earth, and other things. So, since he did not fall short in presenting a ruling in every chapter by which one may infer its analogues from that chapter, how could he altogether refrain from the most important of chapters, not pronouncing on it by an explicit text, nor pointing to a specific person, nor mentioning him by description, until the community remained in disagreement in matters of principles and branches: some are misguided and some are guided, each one claiming that he is right and his opponent is false, with no judge between them; and some are ignorant and some are knowledgeable, each one of them claiming that he is the knowledgeable one and his opponent is ignorant, with no guide for them. For if it is permissible for servants to say, 'Our Lord, if only You had sent us a messenger' (Qur'an 28:47/20:134), and that it is not permissible to question Allah, then may it not be permissible for the community to say: 'Our Lord, if only You had designated for us an imam whose word we could follow before we are humiliated and disgraced?' And that Allah, exalted,
Source pages p. 485

Commentary
This passage presents the second Shi‘a argument for the necessity of an imam, focusing on the Prophet’s duty to designate one. The Shi‘a begin with a principle: we must think well of the Companions, assuming they never deviate from an explicit text (nass) to personal choice, especially the earliest elite (al-safwa al-ula) at the time of fresh acceptance (taraawat al-qabul). By the same reasoning, we must think well of the Prophet: he knew the great need of the people for a leader who unites them, resolves disputes, enforces the law, and does justice. This need is greater than the need for detailed rulings on minor issues like istinja (cleansing after relieving oneself), wiping over socks (mash 'ala al-khuffayn), and tayammum (dry ablution). Since the Prophet did not fail to provide a ruling for every chapter of the law—each ruling serving as an indicator for similar cases—how could he have completely refrained from the most important chapter (i.e., the imamate), neither stating it explicitly, nor pointing to a specific person, nor describing him? The result is that the community remains divided in principles and branches, with some misguided and some guided, each claiming to be right, and no judge between them; some ignorant and some learned, each claiming knowledge, with no guide. Then the Shi‘a draw an analogy: just as it is possible for people on the Day of Judgment to complain that God did not send a messenger (Qur'an 28:47/20:134), and that such a complaint against God is not permissible (because He is not obliged by human questioning), similarly it would be possible for the community to complain: 'Our Lord, why did you not designate for us an imam whose word we could follow before we are humiliated and disgraced?' The passage cuts off with 'wa anna Allaha ta'ala' (and that Allah, exalted...) leaving the conclusion incomplete. The argument implies that God would not leave the community without an imam, just as He did not leave them without a messenger.

Key Terms
حسن الظن
النص
الاختيار
الصفوة الأولى
طراوة القبول
التعيين
الدعوة باللسان والسيف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument claims the Prophet literally forgot to appoint an imam.
Correction: It does not claim forgetfulness; rather, based on 'good opinion' (husn al-zann), it infers that he must have designated an imam, otherwise he would have left a critical need unaddressed.

Checkpoint
In the second Shi'a proof, what is the basis for the analogy that the Prophet must have designated an imam?
A) The Companions were all infallible and they appointed an imam.
B) The Prophet gave detailed rulings for minor issues but could not have omitted the most important need.
C) The Qur'an explicitly names the imam in verses about obedience.
D) The community's disagreement proves that an imam is not needed.

Key Takeaway
The Shi'a argue that the Prophet's thoroughness in even minor legal matters demands that he would not have neglected the crucial issue of leadership.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the Prophet gave such attention to rulings on wiping over socks and dry ablution, how does your own attention to minor religious details compare to your attention to the 'most important' matters? Where are you failing to prioritize as he did?
?
The argument says it is obligatory to think well of the Prophet that he would not neglect a crucial need. Do you extend that same 'good opinion' to yourself? When you fail to act on a pressing duty, do you assume you will somehow be excused?
?
The Shi'a complain about the absence of an imam as a guide. In your own life, who or what do you turn to for guidance when you are divided or confused? Are you truly following a clear guide, or are you left without one because you neglected to seek it?

The Prophet's Failure to Appoint an Imam: Objection and Dilemma
Objection against the Prophet not appointing an imam and a dilemma for the objector.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical dilemma used to refute the objection that the Prophet did not designate an imam, and how the argument traps the objector between impugning the Prophet or the Companions.

Core Idea
The objector cannot avoid blaming either the Prophet or the Companions.

This passage presents a classic kalam rebuttal. An opponent argues that the Prophet should have explicitly designated an imam, but the author turns this into a dilemma: either the Prophet failed to act (impugning his trustworthiness) or the Companions disobeyed (impugning their integrity). The author then dismantles the objector's alternatives—whether the matter was left as a free-for-all or not—showing each leads to contradiction. Read carefully to follow the logical trap.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The objection states that God sent messengers to remove excuses, so the Prophet should have clarified the imamate and named the imam to remove excuses after him. Note the parallel structure: 'as messengers remove excuses, so should imams.'
The objector assumes that designation is necessary to prevent excuse-making after the Prophet.
2
Grasp the dilemma
The author presents a forced choice: either the Prophet knew the need but didn't designate (blame the Prophet) or he designated but the Companions ignored him (blame the Companions). Both options are unacceptable to the objector, who venerates both.
This dilemma shifts the burden back onto the objector.
3
Examine the first alternative: fawḍā
The objector might say the matter was left as a 'free-for-all' for ijtihad. The author responds by analogy: if that were valid, why send messengers? The Companions themselves did not treat it as a free-for-all because they prioritized choosing an imam over burying the Prophet.
This analogy shows inconsistency in the objector's position.
4
Examine the second alternative: naṣṣ
If the objector says it was not a free-for-all, then a clear text (naṣṣ) is required for designation. But only those who claim a text have it; those who don't claim one cannot be designated by it. This leaves the objector without a valid imam.
The objector's own position undermines the imamate of Abu Bakr unless they claim a text for him.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The author forces the objector into a corner: either impiety against the Prophet/Companions or an indefensible position on the imamate. The passage is a masterful use of logical dilemma to defend Sunni doctrine.
This is not a refutation of imamate itself, but of the objection that it was not appointed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أرسل الرسل كيلا يكون للناس على الله حجة بعد الرسل، فهلا بين الرسول الإمامة وعين الإمام كيلا يكون للناس على النبي حجة بعد الأئمة. فلئن قلتم : عرف احتياج الخلق ولم يعين فلم تحسنوا الظن به، وإن قلتم : عين وبين ولم يتبعوا قوله لم تحسنوا الظن بالصحابة، فما الأخلص وما الأولى بتوريك الذنب عليه؟ وأنتم بين أمرين إما أن تقولوا : جعل الأمر بين الأمة فوضى، وفوض الأمر إلى رأي المجتهدين ليبين فضل المجتهد الناظر وقصور القاصر عن رتبة الاجتهاد، وجعل العلماء بجملتهم حملة الشريعة ونقلة الدين. قيل لكم: وهلا كان الأمر فوضى بين العقلاء، وفوض الأمر إلى نظر الناظرين واجتهاد المجتهدين فلا يرسل إليهم رسلا مبشرين ومنذرين ليتبين فضل المجتهد الناظر وقصور المعطل القاصر، وهلا سلك الصحابة هذا المنهاج فيجعلون الأمر فوضى بين الأمة، ولا يرتبون أمر الإمامة، ولا يشتغلون بتعيين الإمام عن مواراة النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم في صريحه ليظهر فضل الفاضل وقصور القاصر وإما أن تقولوا: لم يجعل الأمر فوضى ولا ترك الحال شورى فيلزمكم النص والتعيين، ثم لا نص إلا في حق من يدعي النص، ومن لا يدعي النص كيف يتعين بالنص.
He sent messengers so that people would not have an argument against God after the messengers. So why did the messenger not clarify the imamate and designate the imam so that people would not have an argument against the prophet after the imams? For if you say: He recognized the need of the creatures but did not designate, then you have not thought well of him. And if you say: He designated and clarified but they did not follow his statement, then you have not thought well of the Companions. So which is more sincere and more appropriate to shift the blame onto? And you are between two matters: Either you say: He made the matter a free-for-all among the community and delegated it to the opinion of the mujtahids to show the virtue of the mujtahid observer and the deficiency of the deficient from the rank of ijtihad, and made the scholars as a whole the bearers of the Sharia and transmitters of the religion. It was said to you: Why was the matter not a free-for-all among the rational people, and delegated to the consideration of the thinkers and the ijtihad of the mujtahids, so that he would not send to them messengers as bringers of good tidings and warners to show the virtue of the mujtahid observer and the deficiency of the negligent deficient? And why did the Companions not follow this method and make the matter a free-for-all among the community, and not arrange the matter of imamate, and not busy themselves with designating the imam over concealing the prophet, may God bless him and grant him peace, in his clear statement to show the virtue of the virtuous and the deficiency of the deficient? Or you say: He did not make the matter a free-for-all nor leave the situation as consultation, so the text and designation become necessary for you. Then there is no text except in the right of the one who claims the text, and the one who does not claim the text, how is he designated by the text?
Source pages p. 486

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and a dilemma. The author begins by stating the objection: God sent messengers to remove excuses after them, so why did the Prophet not clarify the imamate and designate the imam? Then he presents a dilemma to the objector: if you say the Prophet recognized the need but did not designate, you impugn his trust; if you say he designated but the Companions did not follow, you impugn the Companions. The author then offers two possible positions for the objector: either the matter was left as a free-for-all (fawḍā) or it was not. He critiques the first position by analogy: if it were left as a free-for-all, why were messengers sent? The Companions themselves did not treat it as a free-for-all because they busied themselves with designating an imam. The second position requires a clear text (naṣṣ) for designation, but only those who claim a text have it, and those who do not claim one cannot be designated by it. Thus the objector is trapped.

Key Terms
الإمامة
النص
الفوضى
الاجتهاد
المجتهد
الصحابة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument is a refutation of the imamate itself, or that it proves the Prophet did not appoint an imam.
Correction: The argument actually rebuts the objection that the Prophet should have appointed an imam explicitly. It defends the Sunni position that the caliphate was established by consensus and ijtihad, not by explicit text.

Checkpoint
What dilemma does the author present to the objector who claims the Prophet did not appoint an imam?
A) Either the Prophet was ignorant or the Companions were disobedient.
B) Either the Prophet recognized the need but did not designate (impugning the Prophet) or he designated but they did not follow (impugning the Companions).
C) Either the matter was left as a free-for-all or it was not.
D) Either the text is clear or it is not.

Key Takeaway
The objection that the Prophet did not appoint an imam is refuted by a dilemma that forces the objector to either impugn the Prophet or the Companions, or adopt an inconsistent position on the nature of religious authority.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you had been present at the Prophet's deathbed, would you have trusted that the community could manage leadership without a clear text, or would you have felt anxious? What does your answer reveal about your reliance on God's wisdom versus your need for clear human leadership?
?
The dilemma forces you to choose between blaming the Prophet or blaming the Companions. When you think of disagreements in your own life about who should lead, do you tend to blame the one who did not design a system or those who did not follow it? How does this reflect your own heart's inclination towards trust or suspicion?
?
Imagine you are one of the Companions after the Prophet's death. Would you prioritize burying the Prophet quickly or designating a leader? Your priority reveals what you truly value. Are you willing to honestly examine your priorities and see if they align with the Companions' actions?

Dismissing Imamiyyah Slanders
Sanusi dismisses Imamiyyah slanders as nonsense.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author dismisses Imamiyyah accusations against God and the Companions as nonsense unworthy of scholarly attention.

Core Idea
Imamiyyah slanders are baseless fabrications, not fit for books or pens.

The author addresses two sectarian claims about imamate. First, he refutes the Imamiyyah by categorically dismissing their negative statements about God and the Companions, as well as their fabricated hadiths. He declares all of it as 'tarahāt' (nonsense) that should not fill books or occupy scholars.

Guided Path
1
Read the first part of the passage
Read the Arabic segment about the Imamiyyah. Notice how the author dismisses their claims in strong terms.
The author uses the word 'tarahāt' (nonsense).
2
Identify what is being dismissed
The author dismisses three things: evil speech about God, evil speech about the Companions, and fabricated hadiths. List them.
3
Reflect on the author's tone
Why does the author say these are not even fit to fill books or practice the pen? Consider how a scholar responds to weak arguments.
This shows the author's confidence in the Sunni position.

واعلم أنه لا شبه في المسألة إلا ما ذكرناه وما تذكره الإمامية من سوء القول في الله الصحابة رضي عنهم، وافتراء الأحاديث على الرسول فكل ذلك ترهات لا يصلح أن تشحن بها الكتب أو تجرب بها القلم، وكذلك ما ذكره الزيدية من إمامة المفضول مع قيام الفاضل واستحقاق الإمامة عندهم بخصال أربع ؛ العفة والعلم والشجاعة، والخروج بعد أن يكون فاطميا، يلزمهم أن يكون في كل صفع إمام واجب الطاعة، فيكون في الأرض ألف ألف إمام نافذ الأمر واجب الطاعة، إذا استجمع كل واحد هذه الخصال.
And know that there is no ambiguity in the matter except what we have mentioned and what the Imamiyyah mention of evil speech about God and the Companions, may God be pleased with them, and fabrication of hadiths upon the Messenger, all that is nonsense that is not fit to fill books or to practice the pen with. Likewise, what the Zaydiyyah mentioned regarding the imamate of the inferior one while the superior exists, and that entitlement to the imamate according to them is by four characteristics: chastity, knowledge, bravery, and rising up after being Fatimid, it necessitates that in every region there be an imam whose obedience is obligatory, so there would be on earth a thousand thousand imams executing command and obligatory obedience, if each one combines these characteristics.
Source pages p. 487

Commentary
This passage refutes the positions of two sects. First, the Imamiyyah: their accusations against God and the Companions and their fabricated hadiths are dismissed as nonsense (tarahāt) not worth writing. Second, the Zaydiyyah: they claim that an inferior person can be imam over a superior, and that the imam must have four qualities: chastity, knowledge, bravery, and uprising ('khurūj') after being Fatimid (descendant of Fatima). The author argues that this would imply an imam in every region, leading to a million imams with obligatory obedience, which is absurd.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The author says these fabrications are not fit for the pen. What nonsense are you allowing to occupy your thoughts and writings—gossip, false stories, or evil speech about others? Will you commit to purging your mind of such tarahaat today?
?
Have you ever dismissed a false accusation as beneath you, or did you feel compelled to defend yourself? Reflect: is your ego more concerned with refuting slander, or with protecting the honor of Allah and His Companions? Ask Allah to grant you wisdom in how you respond to baseless attacks.

Refuting Zaydiyyah Conditions for Imamate
Sanusi reduces Zaydiyyah imamate conditions to absurdity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical absurdity that results from the Zaydiyyah's four conditions for imamate.

Core Idea
Zaydiyyah conditions would create millions of obligatory imams, an impossibility.

After dismissing the Imamiyyah, the author turns to the Zaydiyyah sect. They hold that an inferior person can be imam over a superior, and that the imam must possess four qualities including rising up (khurūj) and being Fatimid. The author points out that this would lead to an absurd consequence: countless imams on earth.

Guided Path
1
Read the Zaydiyyah passage
Read the part about the Zaydiyyah. Identify the four characteristics they require for an imam.
The four are: chastity, knowledge, bravery, and rising up after being Fatimid.
2
Understand the logical consequence
The author argues that if these conditions are met, any region could have an imam, leading to an enormous number of imams. Why is that problematic? Consider that imamate is a single authority.
A 'thousand thousand' means a million imams.
3
Reflect on the reductio ad absurdum
The author uses a reductio ad absurdum: if the conditions are sufficient, there would be too many imams. This exposes the flaw in the Zaydiyyah criteria.

Sunni Proof of Imamate's Obligation via Consensus
Sunni response to Najadat: imamate obligation proven by ijmā' and Companions' actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ahl al-Sunnah proves the obligation of imamate through consensus (ijmā') and the Companions' actions, and how they respond to the Najadat's denial.

Core Idea
The Companions' disagreement over who should be imam proves that having an imam is obligatory.

The Najadat, a Kharijite sect, denied that imamate is obligatory either rationally or by divine law. In this passage, the Sunni scholar responds by grounding obligation in divine law (shar') and specifically in the consensus of the community. He offers a striking argument: the very fact that the Companions disagreed about who should be the imam shows that the principle of having an imam was accepted by all. This lesson unpacks that argument and the methodological issues it raises.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the main claim
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Pay attention to the Sunni position and the proof they offer.
The passage is a response to the Najadat, so note the context.
2
Understand the argument from disagreement
The author argues that the disagreement among the Companions over the designation of the imam is the clearest evidence that the principle of imamate is obligatory. Ask yourself: why does disagreement about who prove that the thing itself is necessary?
This is a key logical move: disagreement about means implies agreement on ends.
3
Consider the anticipated objection
The author raises a possible objection: is consensus itself a valid proof, and can it occur without disagreement? Notice how he responds by showing that consensus is conceivable in matters of rational possibility and that its actual occurrence among the early Companions was easy due to their limited number.
This shows the author's methodological awareness of the need to defend consensus as a source of law.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أجاب أهل السنة عن مقالة النجدات في نفي وجوب الإمامة أصلا عقلا وشرعا أن الواجبات عندنا بالشرع، ومدرك هذا الواجب إجماع الأمة، والاختلاف الذي ذكرتموه في تعيين الإمام من أذل الدليل على أن أصل الإمامة واجب ؛ إذ لو لم يكن واجبا لما شرعوا في التعيين، ولما اشتغلوا به كل الاشتغال. بقي أن يقال: إجماع الأمة هل هو دليل في الشرع؟ وأن الإجماع هل يتصور وقوعه صادرًا عن الاجتهاد بحيث لا يتصور فيه الخلاف؟ وأما تصوره الجائزات العقلية موافقة شخصين على رأي واحد وإذا تصور في شخصين فما المانع من تصوره في ثلاثة وأربعة إلى أن يستوعب الجميع؟ وأما تقدير وقوعه في الصدر الأول فهو أيسر ما في المقدور، فإن الصحابة كانوا محصورين في المهاجرين
The Ahl al-Sunnah answered the statement of the Najadat in denying the obligation of the imamate absolutely, rationally and legally, that obligations according to us are by the Law, and the source of this obligation is the consensus of the community, and the disagreement that you mentioned concerning the designation of the imam is the clearest evidence that the principle of the imamate is obligatory; for if it were not obligatory, they would not have embarked on designation and busied themselves with it so much. It remains to be said: Is the consensus of the community a proof in the Law? And can consensus conceivably occur arising from ijtihad in such a way that disagreement is inconceivable in it? As for its being conceivable in possible matters of reason, it is possible for two persons to agree on one opinion. And if it is conceivable for two, then what prevents it from being conceivable for three, four, until it encompasses all? And as for estimating its occurrence in the first generation, it is the easiest of what is possible, for the Companions were limited to the Muhajirun
Source pages p. 487

Commentary
This passage presents the Sunni response to the Kharijite sect of Najadat, who denied the obligation of imamate altogether, both rationally and by divine law. The author states that obligations are known by the divine law (shar'), and the source of the obligation of imamate is the consensus (ijmā') of the community. He argues that the very disagreement the Najadat mention regarding the designation of the imam is the clearest proof that the principle of imamate is obligatory; if it were not obligatory, the Companions would not have engaged in designating one. The author then anticipates a possible objection: is consensus a valid legal proof, and is it possible for consensus to occur through ijtihad without the possibility of disagreement? He answers that consensus is conceivable in rational possibilities—two people can agree on one opinion, and if that is possible for two, it is possible for three, four, and so on until all agree. Moreover, its actual occurrence in the first generation is the easiest of possibilities, because the Companions were limited to the Muhajirun (and Ansar, implied), making agreement feasible.

Key Terms
أهل السنة
النجدات
وجوب الإمامة
الإجماع
الرأي
الاجتهاد
المهاجرين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Sunni argument relies solely on rational necessity, but it is based on the scriptural proof of consensus and the historical fact of the Companions' actions.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that obligations are by the Law (shar'), not mere reason, and the proof is consensus, which is a scriptural source. The Companions' actions serve as evidence of that consensus.

Checkpoint
How do the Ahl al-Sunnah prove the obligation of imamate according to this passage?
A) By rational necessity alone.
B) By the consensus of the community and the fact that the Companions engaged in designating an imam.
C) By explicit text from the Quran.
D) By the necessity of preventing chaos.

Key Takeaway
The obligation of imamate is proven by the consensus of the community, and the Companions' disagreement over who should be imam actually confirms that the principle itself is obligatory.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly regard the imamah (leadership of the Muslim community) as an obligation upon you today, or have you, like the Najadat, dismissed it as irrelevant? What would change in your priorities if you took it as seriously as the Companions did?
?
When you see disagreements among Muslims about who should lead, do you use that as an excuse to abandon the principle of unity, or does it drive you to seek the truth as the Companions did? Examine your own heart: where do you stand?
?
The Companions busied themselves with the designation of the imam because they knew its necessity. What concrete steps have you taken to understand and uphold that same obligation in your own time and place?

Refutation of Imamiyya and Zaydiyya Claims on the Imamate
The author dismisses Imamiyya and Zaydiyya positions on imamate as baseless.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Al-Shahrastani dismisses the Imamiyya and Zaydiyya criteria for the imamate as untenable, and how his reductio ad absurdum argument applies to the Zaydiyya conditions.

Core Idea
False criteria for imamate lead to logical absurdities and must be rejected.

Al-Shahrastani has already presented the orthodox Sunni position on the imamate. Now he turns to refute two rival sectarian views: the Imamiyya, who impugn the Companions and fabricate hadith, and the Zaydiyya, who set specific conditions for the imam. This passage shows why neither position can stand rationally, and why the Zaydiyya conditions in particular would destroy the unity of the Muslim community.

Guided Path
1
Read the author's dismissal of Imamiyya claims
Notice how the author does not even engage with their arguments; he dismisses them as nonsense (ترهات).
2
Identify the Zaydiyya's four conditions
The author lists the conditions for imamate according to the Zaydiyya: chastity, knowledge, courage, and rising up, and being a Fatimid.
3
Follow the reductio ad absurdum
See how the author applies the Zaydiyya's own criteria to show that if any Fatimid with those qualities is an imam, there would be thousands of imams.
This is a classic reductio argument.
4
Reflect on the implication for Muslim unity
Why does an unlimited number of imams undermine the very concept of a single imamate?
The imamate is meant to unify the community, not fragment it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أنه لا شبه في المسألة إلا ما ذكرناه وما تذكره الإمامية من سوء القول في الله الصحابة رضي عنهم، وافتراء الأحاديث على الرسول فكل ذلك ترهات لا يصلح أن تشحن بها الكتب أو يجرب بها القلم، وكذلك ما ذكره الزيدية من إمامة المفضول مع قيام الفاضل واستحقاق الإمامة عندهم بخصال أربع: العفة والعلم والشجاعة والخروج بعد أن يكون فاطميا، يلزمهم أن يكون في كل صفع إمام واجب الطاعة، فيكون في الأرض ألف ألف إمام نافذ الأمر واجب الطاعة، إذا استجمع كل واحد هذه الخصال.
Know that there is no doubt in the matter except what we have mentioned and what the Imamiyya mention of evil speech concerning Allah and the Companions, may Allah be pleased with them, and fabricating hadiths upon the Messenger — all of that is nonsense that should not fill books or be used to try the pen. Likewise, what the Zaydiyya mentioned of the imamate of the inferior while the superior exists, and their claim that the imamate is deserved by four characteristics: chastity, knowledge, courage, and rising up after being a Fatimid — it follows from them that there is in every region an imam whose obedience is obligatory, so there would be a thousand thousand imams on earth with effective command and obligatory obedience, if each one gathers these characteristics.
Source pages p. 487

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of two sectarian positions. The author first dismisses the Imamiyya's accusations and forgeries as worthless. Then he critiques the Zaydiyya's doctrine that the imam must be a Fatimid with four qualities: chastity, knowledge, courage, and armed revolt. He argues that this would lead to an absurd multiplication of imams wherever such a person exists, undermining the unity of the Muslim community.

الفاضل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the author is rejecting the imamate itself, but he is rejecting only these sects' criteria.
Correction: The author affirms the necessity of the imamate; he only disputes the false conditions set by these sects.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what would the Zaydiyya's conditions for the imamate lead to?
A) The imamate of Abu Bakr
B) An unlimited number of imams
C) The imamate of Ali
D) The abolition of the imamate

Key Takeaway
The only valid criteria for the imamate are those established by the Sunni consensus; sectarian conditions lead to logical and communal absurdity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever set conditions for accepting religious authority that are not from the Quran and Sunnah? This passage shows the absurdity of man-made criteria. In what area of your faith are you most tempted to add or subtract conditions, and how will you return to the original sources?
?
The author dismisses false accusations and forgeries as 'nonsense that should not fill books'. Are there any falsehoods about Islam or its early history that you have passively accepted or repeated without verification? Will you commit to actively refuting them with knowledge?
?
The Zaydiyya’s error came from a seemingly pious requirement (descendant of Fatima, etc.) that led to a logical contradiction. Think of a belief you hold strongly. If you follow it to its logical conclusion, does it lead to an absurdity? How can you test your beliefs against the consensus of the Salaf?

The Obligation of the Imamate: Response to the Najadat
Sunni response to Najadat on imamate obligation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ahl al-Sunnah argue for the obligation of the imamate using consensus and the Companions' actions.

Core Idea
The Companions' disagreement over who becomes imam proves the imamate itself is obligatory.

The Najadat, a Kharijite subsect, claimed that appointing an imam is neither rationally nor legally obligatory. The Ahl al-Sunnah refute this by grounding obligations in revelation and pointing to the consensus of the Muslim community. In this passage, the author presents a clever argument: the very fact that the Companions engaged in selecting a successor—despite disagreeing on who—shows that establishing an imamate is a required duty, not optional.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the structure: the author first states the Sunni position, then gives the proof from consensus, and finally uses the Companions' disagreement as evidence.
The word 'أذل' may be a scribal variant of 'أدل' (most indicative).
2
Identify the Sunni premise
The Ahl al-Sunnah assert that obligations come from shari'ah, not pure reason. The specific obligation of the imamate is known by ijma' (consensus). Understand this premise before moving on.
3
Analyze the proof from disagreement
The author says that if the imamate were not obligatory, the Companions would not have bothered to appoint anyone. Their intense engagement in the matter proves they considered it a duty. This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.
Think about the logical form: 'If not obligatory, then no action; action occurred, therefore obligatory.'
4
Contrast with the Najadat view
The Najadat denied any obligation. The Sunni response shows that the very historical fact of appointment contradicts that denial. Ask yourself: why is consensus considered a valid source of obligation?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أجاب أهل السنة عن مقالة النجدات في نفي وجوب الإمامة أصلا عقلا وشرعا أن الواجبات عندنا بالشرع، ومدرك هذا الواجب إجماع الأمة، والاختلاف الذي ذكرتموه في تعيين الإمام من أذل الدليل على أن أصل الإمامة واجب؛ إذ لو لم يكن واجبا لما شرعوا في التعيين، ولما اشتغلوا به كل الاشتغال.
The Ahl al-Sunnah answered the article of the Najadat in their denial of the obligation of the imamate absolutely, rationally and legally, that obligations according to us are by the Law (shar'), and the source of this obligation is the consensus of the community (ijma' al-ummah). The disagreement you mentioned regarding the appointment of the imam is among the clearest proofs that the imamate is obligatory; for if it were not obligatory, they would not have embarked on appointment, nor would they have engaged in it so completely.
Source pages p. 487

Commentary
This passage is a response to the Najadat (a Kharijite subsect) who deny that the imamate is obligatory either by reason or by law. The Ahl al-Sunnah reply that obligations are known by revelation, and the specific obligation of establishing an imam is known through consensus. The proof is that the Companions engaged in appointing a successor; if the imamate were not obligatory, they would not have done so. Thus the very disagreement about who to appoint shows that the imamate is a required duty.

Key Terms
النجدات
وجوب الإمامة
إجماع الأمة
التعيين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Ahl al-Sunnah response, what evidence is used to show that the imamate is obligatory?
A) The explicit text of the Quran
B) The consensus of the community
C) The practice of the Prophet
D) Rational necessity

Key Takeaway
The imamate is obligatory based on the consensus of the community, demonstrated by the Companions' actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Companions, despite their grief and the shock of the Prophet’s death, immediately engaged in appointing a leader. If you were in their position, would you have had the same sense of urgency? What does your hesitation—if any—reveal about your commitment to the collective duties of the faith?
?
You inherit a community that believes the imamate is obligatory by consensus. Yet today, many Muslims reject political leadership as 'not my problem.' How does your own attitude toward leadership in your community—whether in a masjid, organization, or family—reflect the Companions’ example or the Najadat’s denial?
?
The disagreement over who should be imam is used as proof that the imamate itself is obligatory. When you disagree with others about details of Islamic practice, do you ever use that disagreement to affirm the underlying obligation? Or do you let the disagreement weaken your commitment to the duty itself?
