Defense of the Consensus on Abu Bakr's Caliphate
The author rejects the claim that the consensus on Abu Bakr was not universal, noting Ali's later allegiance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends the completeness of the consensus on Abu Bakr's caliphate by addressing the objection regarding 'Ali's delay.

Core Idea
Even 'Ali's initial delay did not break the consensus.

Some object that the consensus on Abu Bakr's caliphate was not universal because 'Ali did not immediately participate. The author responds that in fact all Companions gave allegiance, and 'Ali's delay was solely due to his involvement in the Prophet's burial. This lesson examines the author's rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The author begins by citing the claim that the agreement on Abu Bakr's imamate was not from all Companions. Recognize that this is the objection being refuted.
The phrase 'As for their statement' indicates a response.
2
Examine the author's rebuttal
The author asserts that not a single Companion remained without giving the pledge. This is a strong counterclaim. Notice the absolute language.
3
Analyze the explanation for 'Ali
The author specifically addresses 'Ali's case: he was occupied with the burial of the Prophet and grieving. This explains his delay without denying eventual allegiance.
The author does not claim 'Ali gave the pledge at that exact moment, but that his situation does not invalidate the consensus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قولهم: إن الاتفاق على إمامة أبي بكر رضي الله عنه لم يكن صادرًا عن جملتهم ليس كذلك؛ إذ لم يبق أحد من الصحابة إلا كانت له بيعة، وعلي كرم الله وجهه كان مشغولا في وقت البيعة بمواراة رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم محزونا.
As for their statement that the agreement on the imamate of Abu Bakr, may Allah be pleased with him, was not from all of them, it is not so; for there remained no Companion except that he gave the pledge (bay'ah), and 'Ali, may Allah honor his face, was occupied at the time of the pledge with the burial of the Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and give him peace, grieving.
Source pages p. 488

Commentary
This passage is a response to an objection that the consensus on Abu Bakr's caliphate was not universal because 'Ali did not initially join. The author argues that in fact every Companion gave the pledge, and 'Ali's delay was due to his immediate involvement in the Prophet's burial, not because he withheld allegiance. Thus the consensus was complete.

Key Terms
أبو بكر
البيعة
علي
مواراة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some assume that 'Ali never gave allegiance to Abu Bakr, or that the consensus was flawed because 'Ali did not participate initially.
Correction: The author clarifies that all Companions, including 'Ali, eventually gave allegiance, and 'Ali's initial absence was due to his involvement in the Prophet's burial.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why did 'Ali not immediately give the pledge to Abu Bakr?
A) He rejected Abu Bakr's caliphate
B) He was occupied with the Prophet's burial
C) He was in a different city
D) He was ill

Key Takeaway
The author defends the consensus on Abu Bakr's caliphate by showing that all Companions gave allegiance, and 'Ali's delay was due to the Prophet's burial, not dissent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
When you face a decision where the majority has already spoken, do you look for reasons to hold back or do you look for ways to join, even if your circumstances are difficult? What would it take for you to prioritize community unity over personal convenience?

Objection and Answer on Obedience Contradiction in Imamate
Refutation of contradiction objection in imamate

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the objection of a contradictory obligation between imam and community is invalid, because the obligation originates from divine revelation (the Lawgiver) and not from human consensus.

Core Idea
Obedience in imamate flows from revelation, not from circular human agreement.

This lesson tackles a clever objection against the doctrine of imamate: if the community's consensus establishes the imam's authority, then the imam must obey the community, yet the community must obey the imam — a contradiction. The author refutes this by clarifying that the true source of obligation is the Lawgiver (Allah and His Prophet), with consensus merely manifesting that obligation. Thus, no contradiction arises.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The objector claims that consensus in appointing the imam creates a mutual obligation that is contradictory: the imam must obey the community, and the community must obey the imam.
This is a common philosophical challenge.
2
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic text once, then read the translation. Notice the flow: objection (قَولُهُم) and answer (نَقُول).
3
Identify the author's key distinction
The author distinguishes the source of obligation: it is not restricted to those who form consensus but is based on the hidden and explicit text of the Lawgiver. Consensus is merely a manifestation, not the origin.
This is the core of the refutation.
4
Reflect on the role of consensus
Consensus is a definitive path to knowing the obligation, but the obligation itself comes from divine revelation. Without this distinction, the contradiction would stand.
5
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to ensure you grasp the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقولهم : إن الإجماع في نصب الإمامة إيجاب على الإمام من جهة المجمعين حتى يصير واجب الطاعة لهم، وينعكس الأمر بعد ذلك حتى يصيروا واجبي الطاعة له وهو متناقض. نقول: هذا لو كان الوجوب الذي يتلقى من الإجماع مقصورًا على من صدر منه الإجماع وليس الأمر كذلك، فإن الوجوب مستند إلى النص الخفي والجلي من صاحب الشرع عليه السلام فهو الموجب والإجماع مظهر الوجوب، وهو طريق قطعي في معرفته، لا أن قول من لم يثبت صدقه ضرورة يكون حجة.
And their statement: 'Consensus in the appointment of the imam imposes an obligation upon the imam from the side of those who reach consensus, so that he becomes obligated to obey them, and then the matter is reversed so that they become obligated to obey him, which is contradictory.' We say: This would be the case if the obligation derived from consensus were restricted to those from whom the consensus proceeded, but it is not so. Indeed, the obligation is based on the hidden and explicit text from the Lawgiver (peace be upon him)—He is the one who imposes the obligation, and consensus is a manifestation of the obligation, and it is a definitive path to knowing it, not that the statement of one whose truthfulness is not established necessarily is a proof.
Source pages p. 489

Commentary
This is an objection-and-answer passage. The objector argues that if consensus establishes an imam, the imam becomes obligated to obey the community (because their consent gave him authority), and then the community becomes obligated to obey the imam—a contradiction. The author's answer distinguishes the source of obligation. He states that the obligation is not derived from the community's consensus itself; rather, it is based on the hidden and explicit text from the Lawgiver (Shari'a). The Lawgiver is the one who imposes the obligation to obey the imam, and consensus merely manifests that obligation—it is a definitive way of knowing it. The author emphasizes that this is not like taking the word of someone whose truthfulness is not established as a proof. Thus, there is no circular contradiction because the obligation originates from divine revelation, not from human agreement.

Key Terms
إجماع
إمامة
وجوب
نص خفي وجلي
صاحب الشرع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that consensus itself creates a binding obligation that flows from the community to the imam and then back, creating a circle of obedience.
Correction: The author clarifies that the real source of obligation is the Lawgiver (divine text), not consensus. Consensus only reveals or manifests that pre-existing obligation.

Checkpoint
How does the author resolve the apparent contradiction that the imam is obligated to obey the community and the community is obligated to obey the imam?
A) By denying the obligation to obey the imam
B) By stating that the obligation comes from the Lawgiver, not from the community
C) By saying the community's consensus is not binding
D) By claiming the imam is infallible

Key Takeaway
The obligation to obey the imam originates from the Lawgiver's text, not from the community's consensus; therefore, there is no circular contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever obeyed a leader or followed a practice simply because others agreed, without asking whether that obedience is rooted in revelation? What must you do to ensure your allegiances are based on divine command, not human consensus?
?
If the Lawgiver's text is the ultimate source of obligation, then examine your life: where have you placed human agreement above divine command? What specific step will you take today to realign your obedience?
?
The objection assumes a circular reasoning in obedience. Is there any area of your life where you have accepted a circular justification (e.g., 'everyone does it, so it's right')? How will you break that cycle using the principle that obligation comes from the Lawgiver alone?

Disagreeing with the Imam in Ijtihad Matters
The author defends the permissibility of a mujtahid disagreeing with the imam in ijtihad matters.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a mujtahid may disagree with the imam in ijtihad matters, based on the principles that taqlid is not allowed for mujtahids and imams are not infallible.

Core Idea
Disagreement in ijtihad is not rebellion; it is rooted in the non-infallibility of imams.

This passage from al-Shahrastani addresses a critical question: Can a mujtahid disagree with the ruling of the imam? The objector suggests that such disagreement might be impermissible, but the author provides a clear answer supported by historical precedents and logical reasoning. As you read, notice how the author distinguishes between disagreeing with the imam's ijtihad and denying his legitimacy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage is an objection-and-answer format.
2
Identify the key arguments
The author gives a direct answer and then supports it with reasoning. Identify the logical steps: why taqlid is not allowed, what kind of disagreement is permissible, and the underlying principle.
The key principle is that imams are not infallible.
3
Examine the historical examples
The author cites examples from Abu Bakr, Umar, and other Companions. Reflect on how these examples illustrate the permissibility of disagreement in ijtihad.
These examples show that even the most authoritative Companions differed in ijtihad.
4
Understand the rationale
The final sentence explains why disagreement is allowed: imams are not required to be infallible. This is a crucial theological point.
If imams were infallible, disagreement would be impermissible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقولهم : لو خالف كل واحد من المجتهدين إمامه في مسألة جاز؟ قلنا : نعم ؛ لأنه مجتهد، كما أن الإمام مجتهد ولا يجوز لمجتهد تقليد المجتهد، وهو لا يخالفه في الإجماع على أنه إمام بعد استناده إلى النص، وإنما يخالفه في مسألة أخرى وهو جائز، أليس أدى اجتهاد أبي بكر إلى قتال أهل الردة ومانعي الزكاة إليه وسبي ذراريهم واغتنام أموالهم، وأدى اجتهاد عمر رضي الله عنه إلى أن يرد إليهم سباياهم، فردها، وكم من مسألة خالفه الصحابة في مسائل الفرائض والديات وإيجاب الرجم، فرجع إلى قولهم وترك اجتهاده، وهذا لأنه لا يجب العصمة للأئمة فيجوز عليهم الخطأ والكبائر، فضلا عن الزلل في الاجتهاد.
And their statement: 'If each of the mujtahids were to disagree with his imam in a matter, is it permissible?' We say: Yes, because he is a mujtahid, just as the imam is a mujtahid, and it is not permissible for a mujtahid to imitate (taqlid) another mujtahid. And he does not disagree with him concerning the consensus that he is an imam after his reliance on the text; rather, he disagrees with him in another matter, and that is permissible. Did not the ijtihad of Abu Bakr lead him to fight the people of apostasy and those who withheld zakat, and to take their children as captives and seize their wealth? And the ijtihad of 'Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) led him to return their captives to them, so he returned them. And how many matters did the Companions disagree with him concerning issues of inheritance, blood money, and the imposition of stoning, so he returned to their opinion and abandoned his ijtihad? This is because it is not necessary that the imams be infallible, so error and major sins are possible for them, let alone mistakes in ijtihad.
Source pages p. 489

Commentary
This is another objection-and-answer passage. The objector asks: if each mujtahid (independent legal scholar) disagrees with his imam (the ruler) in a legal matter, is that permissible? The author answers yes, because the mujtahid is himself a mujtahid, and the imam is also a mujtahid; one mujtahid may not blindly imitate (taqlid) another. However, the mujtahid does not disagree with the consensus that the imam is the legitimate imam based on textual evidence (nass). Rather, he disagrees with the imam on a specific legal issue, which is permissible. The author supports this with historical examples: the different ijtihad of Abu Bakr and 'Umar regarding the treatment of apostates and captives, and instances where the Companions disagreed with 'Umar on inheritance, diya (blood money), and stoning, and 'Umar accepted their views. The underlying reason is that imams are not infallible (ma'sum); they can err and commit major sins, so mistakes in ijtihad are even more likely. Thus, disagreement in ijtihad is allowed and does not undermine the imam's authority.

Key Terms
مجتهد
إمام
تقليد
عصمة
خطأ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Disagreeing with the imam in any matter is rebellion and impermissible.
Correction: The author clarifies that disagreement in ijtihad matters is permissible because imams are not infallible and taqlid is not allowed for mujtahids.

Checkpoint
Why is it permissible for a mujtahid to disagree with the imam according to the author?
A) Because the imam is not infallible
B) Because the imam's opinion is not based on text
C) Because consensus is not binding
D) Because the mujtahid is superior to the imam

Key Takeaway
A mujtahid may disagree with the imam in ijtihad matters because imams are not infallible and taqlid is not permissible for mujtahids.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever chosen to follow someone's opinion simply because of their status or authority, even when your own reasoning suggested otherwise? What would it take for you to respectfully disagree with a leader or scholar you admire, especially when you have knowledge to back your view?
?
This lesson shows that even the greatest Companions (Abu Bakr and Umar) had differing ijtihad, and each accepted the other's view. Is there someone in your life whom you have dismissed or judged because of a disagreement? Will you now examine your heart and ask: was that disagreement rooted in a valid difference of understanding, or in your own pride?
?
The imams are not infallible, yet we often treat religious leaders as if they can never be wrong. How does this lesson challenge you to take more personal responsibility for your own learning and decision-making, instead of blindly relying on others? What is one area of your faith where you need to become a 'mujtahid' rather than a 'muqallid'?

Refuting the Circularity Objection
Author refutes the objection that consensus creates contradictory obligations in imamate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Shahrastani refutes the objection that consensus in imamate creates a contradictory obligation cycle.

Core Idea
Consensus reveals divine obligation, it does not create it.

A common objection against the Sunnī view of imamate is that if the obligation to obey the imam comes from the community's consensus, then the community first obligates the imam to obey them, then the imam obligates them – a contradiction. Al-Shahrastani responds by clarifying that obligation originates from divine text, not from consensus itself. Let's read his precise argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection as presented
Start by reading the objection carefully. The opponents claim that consensus leads to a flip-flop of obligations.
Notice the phrase 'وإنعكس الأمر بعد ذلك' – the reversal of obligation.
2
Identify the author's conditional response
The author says 'هذا لو كان...' – this would be true IF obligation came from consensus alone. That's the key: he denies the premise.
3
Find the source of obligation
The author states that obligation is based on the hidden and manifest text (nass) from the Lawgiver. Consensus is only a manifestation of that obligation.
The term 'مظهر' is crucial – consensus manifests, not creates.
4
Understand the role of consensus
Consensus is a definitive way (tariq qat'i) to know the obligation, not an independent source. Thus, no contradiction arises.
The author also notes that a non-prophet's statement cannot be a proof by itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقولهم : إن الإجماع في نصب الإمامة إيجاب على الإمام من جهة المجمعين حتى يصير واجب الطاعة لهم، وينعكس الأمر بعد ذلك حتى يصيروا واجبي الطاعة له وهو متناقض. نقول: هذا لو كان الوجوب الذي يتلقى من الإجماع مقصورًا على من صدر منه الإجماع وليس الأمر كذلك، فإن الوجوب مستند إلى النص الخفي والجلي من صاحب الشرع عليه السلام فهو الموجب والإجماع مظهر الوجوب، وهو طريق قطعي في معرفته، لا أن قول من لم يثبت صدقه ضرورة يكون حجة.
And their statement: 'Indeed, consensus in establishing the imamate makes the imam obligated from the side of those who form the consensus, so that he becomes obligated to obey them, and then the matter reverses so that they become obligated to obey him, which is contradictory.' We say: This would be if the obligation derived from consensus were confined to those from whom the consensus issued, but it is not so. For the obligation is based on the hidden and manifest text (nass) from the Lawgiver (peace be upon him); He is the obligator, and consensus is a manifestation of the obligation, and it is a definitive way of knowing it, not that the statement of one whose truthfulness is not necessarily established is a proof.
Source pages p. 489

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer unit. The objectors argue that if the obligation to obey the imam comes from the consensus of the community, then initially the imam is obligated to obey the community (since the consensus made him imam), but then the community becomes obligated to obey the imam. This, they claim, is contradictory. The author answers by distinguishing the source of obligation: it does not originate from the consensus itself but from the divine text (nass) of the Lawgiver, which may be explicit or implicit. Consensus merely reveals or manifests that obligation; it is a definitive method (tariq qat'i) for knowing the ruling, not a self-standing source. Therefore, there is no reversal of obligation from the same source, and the contradiction disappears. The author further notes that the statement of someone whose truthfulness is not necessarily established (i.e., a non-prophet) cannot be a proof by itself.

Key Terms
إجماع
إمامة
نص
وجوب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Consensus is an independent source of obligation that creates a binding rule by itself.
Correction: Consensus only manifests the obligation already established by divine text; it is a means of knowing, not a cause of obligation.

Checkpoint
According to the author, where does the obligation to obey the imam actually originate?
A) From the consensus of the community
B) From the hidden or clear text (nass) of the Lawgiver
C) From the imam's own authority
D) From the people's choice

Key Takeaway
The obligation to obey the imam comes from divine text, not from consensus; consensus only reveals that obligation, resolving the apparent contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you obey a religious authority or follow a consensus, do you consciously refer that obedience back to God as the ultimate obligator, or do you passively accept human decisions as final? What would change if you constantly reminded yourself that obligation is from His text?
?
Have you ever felt trapped in a contradiction between two authorities—like community expectation and divine command? How does the author's distinction between source and manifestation help you resolve such tensions in your own life?
?
If consensus is only a manifestation of obligation, not its origin, then how seriously do you take it? Are you willing to submit to a consensus even when it contradicts your personal inclination, knowing it is a definitive way to know God's will? What is one specific area where you need to realign your obedience?

Mujtahid Imams May Differ and Are Not Infallible
Author explains that a mujtahid may oppose the imam in ijtihad, using examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a mujtahid may oppose the imam in ijtihad and why imams are not considered infallible.

Core Idea
Imams are mujtahids, not infallible; opposing them in ijtihad is permissible.

This lesson tackles a common objection: if a mujtahid opposes his imam, does that undermine the imam's authority? The author answers with a clear principle: both are mujtahids, and a mujtahid should not blindly imitate another. He then supports this with historical examples from Abu Bakr and 'Umar, showing that Companions often differed from the imam. The key takeaway is that imams are not required to be infallible; they can err.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Start by reading the objection posed by the opponents and the author's initial answer: 'Yes, because he is a mujtahid.' Notice the concise logic.
The objection assumes that opposing the imam is problematic.
2
Understand the distinction
The author distinguishes between opposing the imam's status (which is agreed upon) and differing in a specific ruling. This is crucial to resolving the tension.
The imam's authority is not absolute in every detail.
3
Examine the historical examples
Review the examples of Abu Bakr and 'Umar. Notice that they arrived at different ijtihad on the same issue (apostates). This shows legitimate difference.
The Companions also opposed 'Umar on inheritance and stoning, and he accepted their view.
4
Grasp the underlying principle
The author concludes that infallibility ('isma) is not required for imams. Therefore, error in ijtihad is possible. This justifies the permissibility of opposition.
This principle is central to Sunni political and legal thought.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقولهم : لو خالف كل واحد من المجتهدين إمامه في مسألة جاز؟ قلنا : نعم ؛ لأنه مجتهد، كما أن الإمام مجتهد ولا يجوز لمجتهد تقليد المجتهد، وهو لا يخالفه في الإجماع على أنه إمام بعد استناده إلى النص، وإنما يخالفه في مسألة أخرى وهو جائز، أليس أدى اجتهاد أبي بكر إلى قتال أهل الردة ومانعي الزكاة إليه وسبي ذراريهم واغتنام أموالهم، وأدى اجتهاد عمر رضي الله عنه إلى أن يرد إليهم سباياهم ،فردها، وكم من مسألة خالفه الصحابة في مسائل الفرائض والديات وإيجاب الرجم، فرجع إلى قولهم وترك اجتهاده، وهذا لأنه لا يجب العصمة للأئمة فيجوز عليهم الخطأ والكبائر، فضلا عن الزلل في الاجتهاد.
And their statement: 'If every one of the mujtahids opposed his imam in a matter, would it be permissible?' We say: Yes, because he is a mujtahid, just as the imam is a mujtahid, and it is not permissible for a mujtahid to imitate another mujtahid. And he does not oppose him regarding the consensus that he is imam after his reliance on the text, but rather he opposes him in another matter, and that is permissible. Did not the ijtihad of Abu Bakr lead to fighting the people of apostasy and those who withheld zakat, to capturing their offspring and seizing their wealth, and the ijtihad of 'Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) led him to return their captives, so he returned them? How many matters did the Companions oppose him in, regarding questions of inheritance, blood-money, and the imposition of stoning, so he reverted to their opinion and abandoned his ijtihad. And this is because infallibility is not required for the imams, so error and major sins are possible for them, let alone slips in ijtihad.
Source pages p. 489

Commentary
This passage is another objection-and-answer. The objectors ask: if each mujtahid (including the imam) could oppose the imam in a ruling, would that be permissible? The author answers affirmatively, because both are mujtahids, and a mujtahid cannot blindly imitate another mujtahid. The opposition is not about the imam's status as imam (which is established by consensus based on text), but about a specific ruling in another matter. The author then provides historical examples: Abu Bakr's ijtihad led him to fight apostates and take captives, while 'Umar's ijtihad led him to return those captives. The Companions often opposed 'Umar on legal issues like inheritance and stoning, and he deferred to them. The underlying principle is that imams are not required to be infallible (ma'sum); they can err in judgment and even commit major sins, let alone minor mistakes in ijtihad.

Key Terms
اجتهاد
مجتهد
عصمة
إمام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Opposing the imam in a ruling invalidates his authority or is always forbidden.
Correction: The author distinguishes between opposing the imam's status (which is agreed upon) and differing in ijtihad, which is permissible because both are mujtahids and imams are not infallible.

Checkpoint
Why does the author permit a mujtahid to oppose his imam in a legal ruling?
A) Because the imam is not infallible and both are mujtahids
B) Because the imam has no authority
C) Because consensus allows it
D) Because the mujtahid is superior

Key Takeaway
Mujtahid imams are not infallible, so legitimate differences in ijtihad are permissible and do not undermine their authority.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

How does this lesson's teaching that even the imam can err affect how you approach such disagreements? Will you now examine evidence for yourself rather than blindly follow? Commit to reading one source before accepting a ruling.

The Companions opposed 'Umar and he accepted. Have you ever been in a position where you had to change your view based on evidence from someone else? Are you humble enough to accept correction, or do you cling to your opinion? This lesson demands you be open to truth even from those you consider subordinate.
?

This lesson forces you to rethink the level of scrutiny you apply to those you follow. You cannot outsource your responsibility to know right from wrong.

Rational Possibility vs. Human Need for an Imam
Objection and answer on the necessity of an imam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author balances rational possibility and practical necessity regarding the need for an imam.

Core Idea
Reason allows no imam, but human nature demands one.

The question of whether an imam is necessary often meets with the objection that if people were just, they could do without. The author does not dismiss this as false; instead, he reframes it. He shows that while reason does not compel an imam, the reality of human behaviour makes one indispensable. This lesson unpacks this nuanced argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice that the author first states the objection, then gives his answer.
The objection is presented as 'their statement'.
2
Identify the two parts of the answer
The author concedes rational possibility but then argues from custom and practice. Find where he says 'ja'iz fi al-'aql' and where he turns to 'al-'ada al-jariya'.
3
Understand the role of the imam
The author says people need a driver who uses intimidation and severity. Reflect on why human nature makes this necessary.
Consider the contrast between ideal justice and actual human behaviour.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقولهم : إن الناس لو تناصفوا وعدلوا استغنوا عن إمام، قلنا : هذا جائز في العقل جواز سداد الناظرين في نظرهم قبل ورود الشرع، ولكن العادة الجارية والسنة المطردة أن الناس بأنفسهم لا يستقرون على مناهج العدل والشرع إلا بحامل يحملهم على ذلك بالتخويف والتشديد ولا يتأتى ذلك إلا بسياسة الإمام والتخويف بالسيف والتشديد على الظالم.
And their statement: 'If people were upright and just, they would be independent of an imam.' We say: This is rationally possible, like the possibility of the soundness of the opinions of those who reflect before the coming of the law. But the prevailing custom and established practice is that people by themselves do not settle upon the paths of justice and law except by a driver who drives them to that through intimidation and severity, and that is not achieved except through the policy of the imam and intimidation with the sword and severity against the oppressor.
Source pages p. 490

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objectors claim that if people were just and fair, they would not need an imam. The author concedes that this is rationally possible (ja'iz fi al-'aql), analogous to the possibility of sound independent reasoning before revelation. However, he argues that the actual custom ('ada jariya) and established practice (sunna muttarida) show that people do not maintain justice and law on their own without a forceful driver. This driver is the imam, who uses intimidation (takhwif) and severity (tashdid), including the sword, to enforce justice against oppressors. Thus, while reason does not strictly necessitate an imam, human nature and social reality make it indispensable.

Key Terms
إمام
عادة
سياسة
جواز
تخويف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the author denies the rational possibility or rejects the objection entirely.
Correction: The author affirms the rational possibility (ja'iz) but argues that practical custom and human nature make the imam necessary.

Checkpoint
What is the author's stance on the necessity of an imam if people were perfectly just?
A) It would still be obligatory by reason
B) It would be rationally possible but human custom requires an imam
C) It would be unnecessary
D) It would be forbidden

Key Takeaway
While reason does not obligate an imam if humans were angels, the actual human condition makes an imam with authority essential for justice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time when you acted justly only because you feared being caught or punished. If you knew no one would ever find out, would you have acted the same? What does that reveal about your own reliance on external authority?
?
The author says people by themselves do not settle on justice without a driver. Where in your life do you see yourself resisting justice or fairness until some pressure forces you? Will you change that today even if no one is watching?
?
If you were put in a position of authority tomorrow, would you rely on intimidation and severity to enforce justice? How would you balance that with mercy, knowing that your own heart also needs correction?

Refuting Shiite Claims on Textual Designation of the Imam
Author refutes Shiite arguments for textual imam designation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author argues that the imam cannot be designated by a specific text from the Lawgiver, but must be determined by consensus.

Core Idea
If an explicit text existed for the imam, it would have ended all disputes.

Building on earlier arguments about the basis of obligation, the author now directly engages Shiite claims that the imam must be explicitly designated by a divine text. He accepts the general obligation to obey the imam but argues that the specific identity of the imam is not textually specified. This passage provides a logical argument from transmission: had a text existed, it would have been universally known and prevented disagreements like those between the Ansar and Quraysh, and later between Quraysh and Banu Hashim.

Guided Path
1
Read the Shiite claims being addressed
The Shi'a present two arguments: (1) obligations come from reason, and (2) the imamate is based on consensus indicating a text. Note how the author summarizes these before responding.
The author has already dealt with the reason-based argument elsewhere.
2
Identify the author's key distinction
He accepts the obligation to obey the imam but shifts the discussion to 'ta'yin' (specific designation). Understand why this distinction matters – it frames the entire rebuttal.
The Quranic command is about obedience, not about who specifically to obey.
3
Trace the argument from transmission
The author argues that if a text specifying the imam existed, it would have been transmitted and known by all, preventing disputes. He uses the example of the Quraysh text from Abu Bakr's time to illustrate.
The same logic applies to later disputes – silence proves absence of text.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this argument undermines the Shiite position: without a transmitted text, designation must be by consensus, not divine appointment of a specific individual.
This is a classic Sunni argument against the necessity of a designated imam by nass.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الجواب عن قول الشيعة : أما أن تلقى الواجبات من العقل فقد فرغنا منه، وأما مستند الوجوب في نصب الإمامة هو الإجماع الدال على النص الوارد من الشرع. وقولهم : إن الله تبارك وتعالى أمرنا بطاعة أولي الأمر ومتابعة الصادقين، قلنا : هذا مسلم في وجوب طاعة الإمام على الإطلاق، لكن الكلام إنما وقع في التعيين أهو متعين بتعيين الشارع نصا أم يتعين بتعيين أهل الإجماع ؟ والأول لم يثبت إذ لو ثبت لنقل، ولما تصور سكوت القوم أو سكوت واحد من القوم في موضع اختلاف الناس في تعيين الإمام، أليس لما كان عند أبي بكر رضي الله عنه نص في تخصيص قريش بالإمامة روي ذلك في حال دعوى الأنصار فسكتوا عن الدعوى وصارت الإمامة مخصوصة بقريش نصا، كذلك لو كان عند أحد نص في تخصيص بني هاشم لنقل ذلك حتى يرتفع النزاع، فإن منازعة الأنصار قريشا كمنازعة قريش بني هاشم، ومنازعة بني هاشم عليا.
As for the response to the statement of the Shi'a: 'As for deriving obligations from reason, we have already finished with that; and as for the basis of the obligation to establish the imamate, it is consensus indicating the text that came from the Shari'a.' And their statement: 'Allah, blessed and exalted, commanded us to obey those in authority and to follow the truthful ones.' We say: This is accepted regarding the absolute obligation to obey the imam, but the discussion is about the specific designation: is he specified by the Lawgiver's designation by a text, or is he specified by the designation of the people of consensus? The first has not been established, for if it were established it would have been transmitted, and it is inconceivable that the community or a single person from the community would remain silent in a situation where people differed over the designation of the imam. Did not Abu Bakr (may Allah be pleased with him) have a text specifying the Quraysh for the imamate, which was narrated when the Ansar claimed it, so they abandoned their claim, and the imamate became specifically for the Quraysh by text? Similarly, if anyone had a text specifying the Banu Hashim, it would have been transmitted so that the dispute could be resolved, for the dispute of the Ansar with the Quraysh is like the dispute of the Quraysh with the Banu Hashim, and the dispute of the Banu Hashim with 'Ali.
Source pages p. 490

Commentary
This passage is a response to two Shiite arguments. First, the Shi'a claim that obligations (wajibat) are derived from reason ('aql) and that the basis for the obligation to appoint an imam is consensus indicating a text from the Shari'a. The author says they have already dealt with the reason-based argument (presumably earlier), and he reiterates that the basis is consensus pointing to a text. Second, the Shi'a cite the Quranic command to obey those in authority (ulu al-amr) and follow the truthful ones (al-sadiqin). The author accepts this as a general obligation to obey the imam, but notes that the real issue is the specific designation (ta'yin): is the imam explicitly appointed by a text (nass) from the Lawgiver, or is he designated by the consensus of the community? He argues that the first (textual designation) has not been established, because if it had been, it would have been transmitted and known, and the community would not have remained silent during disputes over the imam's identity. He gives the example of Abu Bakr: there was a specific text (presumably the hadith 'the imams are from Quraysh') that was narrated when the Ansar claimed the imamate, so they gave up their claim. Similarly, if there were a text specifying the Banu Hashim (the Prophet's clan) or 'Ali specifically, it would have been transmitted, and disputes would have ended. The author compares the Ansar's dispute with Quraysh to Quraysh's dispute with Banu Hashim, implying that the lack of a transmitted text for Banu Hashim means no such text exists.

Key Terms
شيعة
تعيين
نص
إجماع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some Shiites believe that the imam must be explicitly designated by a text (nass) from the Prophet, and that such a text exists for 'Ali.
Correction: The author argues that if such a text existed, it would have been universally transmitted and would have prevented historical disputes. The lack of transmission and the presence of disputes (like the Ansar vs. Quraysh) prove that no such text exists.

Checkpoint
How does the author counter the Shiite claim that the imam must be textually designated?
A) He argues that no such text exists, as evidenced by the silence of the companions
B) He says the text is hidden
C) He agrees but says the text is metaphorical
D) He says the imam is chosen by the people

Key Takeaway
The absence of a transmitted text designating a specific imam proves that no such text exists, so the imam is designated by consensus, not by divine appointment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a deeply held belief that claims divine authority, are you willing to apply the same standard of evidence to it as you would to any other historical claim? What would it cost you to question whether that belief is truly transmitted from the Prophet?
?
If you were an early Muslim living through the dispute over leadership, would you have remained silent if you knew of a text designating a specific imam? This passage says silence is inconceivable—does your own conscience agree?
?
This argument rests on the principle that important matters would be widely known. Is there any belief you hold that you cannot trace back to a reliable transmission? Are you willing to examine it with the same rigor as the author examines the Shiite claim?

Why an Imam is Needed: Refuting Self-Sufficiency
Response to the objection that self-sufficiency negates the need for an imam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author argues that an imam is practically necessary despite the rational possibility of a just society without one.

Core Idea
Human nature requires a coercive imam to enforce justice.

The first objection against the necessity of an imam suggests that if people were just and fair, they would not need one. In response, the author distinguishes between rational possibility and actual human behavior. This lesson unpacks that distinction and the role of the imam as a practical necessity rooted in custom and experience.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and translation, noting the structure: objection followed by the author's response.
The author begins with 'Qulna' (we say) to signal the response.
2
Identify the two parts of the response
First, the author concedes rational possibility. Second, he invokes 'custom' (al-‘adah) and 'consistent practice' (al-sunnah al-muttaridah) to argue that people cannot achieve justice without a coercive leader.
Notice the shift from rational to customary argument.
3
Understand the role of the imam's authority
The imam provides intimidation and severity, especially against the oppressive, to enforce justice. This is a practical, not theoretical, necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقولهم : إن الناس لو تناصفوا وعدلوا استغنوا عن إمام، قلنا : هذا جائز في العقل جواز سداد الناظرين في نظرهم قبل ورود الشرع، ولكن العادة الجارية والسنة المطردة أن الناس بأنفسهم لا يستقرون على مناهج العدل والشرع إلا بحامل يحملهم على ذلك بالتخويف والتشديد ولا يتأتى ذلك إلا بسياسة الإمام والتخويف بالسيف والتشديد على الظالم.
وأما الجواب عن قول الشيعة : أما أن تلقى الواجبات من العقل فقد فرغنا منه، وأما مستند الوجوب في نصب الإمامة هو الإجماع الدال على النص الوارد من الشرع.
وقولهم : إن الله تبارك وتعالى أمرنا بطاعة أولي الأمر ومتابعة الصادقين، قلنا : هذا مسلم في وجوب طاعة الإمام على الإطلاق، لكن الكلام إنما وقع في التعيين أهو متعين بتعيين الشارع نصا أم يتعين بتعيين أهل الإجماع ؟ والأول لم يثبت إذ لو ثبت لنقل، ولما تصور سكوت القوم أو سكوت واحد من القوم في موضع اختلاف الناس في تعيين الإمام، أليس لما كان عند أبي بكر رضي الله عنه نص في تخصيص قريش بالإمامة روي ذلك في حال دعوى الأنصار فسكتوا عن الدعوى وصارت الإمامة مخصوصة بقريش نصا، كذلك لو كان عند أحد نص في تخصيص بني هاشم لنقل ذلك حتى يرتفع النزاع، فإن منازعة الأنصار قريشا كمنازعة قريش بني هاشم، ومنازعة بني هاشم عليا.
And their saying: 'If people were just and fair, they would be independent of an imam,' we say: This is possible in reason just as the correctness of the two observers in their view before the coming of the Law, but the prevailing custom and consistent practice is that people do not settle upon the paths of justice and law by themselves except by a carrier who carries them to that through intimidation and severity, and that is only achieved through the policy of the imam and intimidation by the sword and severity against the oppressor. As for the answer to the saying of the Shi'a: 'Either the obligations are from reason, we have already finished it, and the basis of obligation in appointing the imamate is the consensus indicating the text from the Law.' And their saying: 'Indeed Allah, blessed and exalted, commanded us to obey those in authority and follow the truthful,' we say: This is conceded regarding the obligation of obedience to the imam absolutely, but the discussion is only about specification: is he specified by a textual specification of the Lawgiver or by specification of the people of consensus? The first is not established, for if it were established it would have been transmitted, and it is not conceivable that the people or a single one of them would be silent in a place where people differ over specifying the imam. Is it not that when Abu Bakr, may Allah be pleased with him, had a text specifying Quraysh for the imamate, it was reported in the case of the Ansar's claim, so they abandoned the claim, and the imamate became specific to Quraysh by text? Similarly, if anyone had a text specifying Banu Hashim, it would have been transmitted so that the dispute would be resolved. Indeed, the dispute of the Ansar with Quraysh is like the dispute of Quraysh with Banu Hashim, and the dispute of Banu Hashim with Ali.
Source pages p. 490

Commentary
This passage presents two objections against the Sunni position on the imamate and responds to them. First, the objection that if people were just they would not need an imam. The answer accepts the rational possibility but argues that in practice, human nature requires a coercive leader with authority to enforce justice and intimidate wrongdoers. This is based on custom and experience. Second, the objection citing the Quranic command to obey those in authority. The response concedes the obligation of obedience to the imam but distinguishes between the general obligation and the specific designation of the imam. The question is whether the imam is designated by an explicit text from the Prophet or by consensus of the community. The author argues that an explicit text for anyone other than Abu Bakr (and the general specification of Quraysh) was never transmitted, using the example of Abu Bakr's time when the Ansar's claim was silenced by the report that the imamate is from Quraysh. If there had been a text for Banu Hashim or Ali, it would have been transmitted, just as the Quraysh text was. The analogical argument shows consistency in the Sunni position that only the Quraysh specification is transmitted, not a specific person.

Key Terms
الإمامة
العادة
التخويف
السيف
العدل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the author denies the rational possibility of a self-sufficient society entirely.
Correction: The author explicitly says it is rationally possible ('ja'iz fi al-‘aql'), but argues that it is not realized in practice due to human nature and custom.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is an imam necessary despite the rational possibility of a just society?
A) Because reason itself demands it
B) Because human nature requires coercion to follow justice
C) Because the Law obligates it directly
D) Because the Prophet explicitly commanded it

Key Takeaway
The imam is a practical necessity because human nature, left to itself, does not consistently follow justice; only a coercive authority can enforce it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you were in a position of absolute power with no accountability, would you still uphold justice? This passage forces you to admit that you—like everyone—need external checks. What does that reveal about your self-image?
?
Think of a time you chose injustice because you knew you could get away with it. How does this passage's claim that people require intimidation and severity confront your own moral weakness?
?
Do you truly believe that society could function without a coercive government? What would happen if everyone acted solely on their sense of justice, as you do? This lesson dares you to examine your trust in human nature—including your own.

Text or Consensus? The Sunni Argument on Imam's Designation
Refutation of the Shi'a argument for textual designation of the imam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni position that the imam is designated by consensus (ijma) rather than an explicit text (nass) for a specific person, and the argument from transmission.

Core Idea
No explicit text designates a specific imam; only the general specification of Quraysh is transmitted.

The second objection involves the Shi'a claim that the imam must be designated by an explicit text. The author responds by distinguishing the general obligation to obey an imam from the specific designation. He argues that if such a text existed for Ali or Banu Hashim, it would have been transmitted like the text for Quraysh. This lesson examines that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation, noting the two parts: first about the basis of obligation (consensus indicating text), and then the command to obey.
2
Identify the distinction between general and specific
The author concedes the general obligation to obey the imam but says the dispute is about specification (ta'yin): is the imam specified by a nass from the Lawgiver or by consensus?
The key Arabic term here is 'al-ta'yin'.
3
Understand the transmission argument
The author claims that if a nass existed for a specific person (e.g., Ali), it would have been transmitted like the text specifying Quraysh. Since it was not, it does not exist.
The analogy with Abu Bakr's time illustrates this: the Quraysh text was transmitted and silenced the Ansar.
4
Draw the analogy
The dispute of the Ansar with Quraysh is compared to the dispute of Quraysh with Banu Hashim, and Banu Hashim with Ali. Consistency demands that if a text existed, it would be transmitted.
This shows the logic: no text for a specific person, only for the tribe of Quraysh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقولهم : إن الناس لو تناصفوا وعدلوا استغنوا عن إمام، قلنا : هذا جائز في العقل جواز سداد الناظرين في نظرهم قبل ورود الشرع، ولكن العادة الجارية والسنة المطردة أن الناس بأنفسهم لا يستقرون على مناهج العدل والشرع إلا بحامل يحملهم على ذلك بالتخويف والتشديد ولا يتأتى ذلك إلا بسياسة الإمام والتخويف بالسيف والتشديد على الظالم.
وأما الجواب عن قول الشيعة : أما أن تلقى الواجبات من العقل فقد فرغنا منه، وأما مستند الوجوب في نصب الإمامة هو الإجماع الدال على النص الوارد من الشرع.
وقولهم : إن الله تبارك وتعالى أمرنا بطاعة أولي الأمر ومتابعة الصادقين، قلنا : هذا مسلم في وجوب طاعة الإمام على الإطلاق، لكن الكلام إنما وقع في التعيين أهو متعين بتعيين الشارع نصا أم يتعين بتعيين أهل الإجماع ؟ والأول لم يثبت إذ لو ثبت لنقل، ولما تصور سكوت القوم أو سكوت واحد من القوم في موضع اختلاف الناس في تعيين الإمام، أليس لما كان عند أبي بكر رضي الله عنه نص في تخصيص قريش بالإمامة روي ذلك في حال دعوى الأنصار فسكتوا عن الدعوى وصارت الإمامة مخصوصة بقريش نصا، كذلك لو كان عند أحد نص في تخصيص بني هاشم لنقل ذلك حتى يرتفع النزاع، فإن منازعة الأنصار قريشا كمنازعة قريش بني هاشم، ومنازعة بني هاشم عليا.
And their saying: 'If people were just and fair, they would be independent of an imam,' we say: This is possible in reason just as the correctness of the two observers in their view before the coming of the Law, but the prevailing custom and consistent practice is that people do not settle upon the paths of justice and law by themselves except by a carrier who carries them to that through intimidation and severity, and that is only achieved through the policy of the imam and intimidation by the sword and severity against the oppressor. As for the answer to the saying of the Shi'a: 'Either the obligations are from reason, we have already finished it, and the basis of obligation in appointing the imamate is the consensus indicating the text from the Law.' And their saying: 'Indeed Allah, blessed and exalted, commanded us to obey those in authority and follow the truthful,' we say: This is conceded regarding the obligation of obedience to the imam absolutely, but the discussion is only about specification: is he specified by a textual specification of the Lawgiver or by specification of the people of consensus? The first is not established, for if it were established it would have been transmitted, and it is not conceivable that the people or a single one of them would be silent in a place where people differ over specifying the imam. Is it not that when Abu Bakr, may Allah be pleased with him, had a text specifying Quraysh for the imamate, it was reported in the case of the Ansar's claim, so they abandoned the claim, and the imamate became specific to Quraysh by text? Similarly, if anyone had a text specifying Banu Hashim, it would have been transmitted so that the dispute would be resolved. Indeed, the dispute of the Ansar with Quraysh is like the dispute of Quraysh with Banu Hashim, and the dispute of Banu Hashim with Ali.

Commentary
This passage presents two objections against the Sunni position on the imamate and responds to them. First, the objection that if people were just they would not need an imam. The answer accepts the rational possibility but argues that in practice, human nature requires a coercive leader with authority to enforce justice and intimidate wrongdoers. Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the Sunni position denies any textual basis for the imamate.
Correction: The Sunni position acknowledges a text specifying Quraysh as a tribe but denies a text specifying individual imams.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why would an explicit text (nass) for a specific imam have been transmitted if it existed?
A) Because the companions were required to transmit all texts
B) Because the dispute about the imam was so significant that it would have been cited
C) Because the Quran commands transmission
D) Because the Shi'a would have preserved it

Key Takeaway
The Sunni position is that the imam is designated by consensus (ijma) based on the transmitted text that the imam must be from Quraysh, not by a text naming a specific person.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever accepted a religious claim without asking whether it has been reliably transmitted? This lesson shows that if a claim were true, it would have been passed down. Is there a belief you hold that you have not verified through transmitted evidence? What would it take for you to abandon it if no evidence exists?
?
When you disagree with a fellow Muslim about a matter of religion, do you search for the evidence as the Companions did at Saqifa, or do you simply insist on your position? Are you willing to change your view when you see clear evidence, even if it challenges your previous assumptions?
?
The Ansar abandoned their claim the moment the Quraysh text was recited. Is there a belief or practice you cling to that, if you were honest, has no solid evidence from revelation or transmitted tradition? What would it take for you to surrender it before Allah?

The Quraysh Hadith and the Ansar's Acceptance
Author remarks on the Quraysh hadith's transmission and Ansar's submission.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Quraysh hadith's non-mutawatir status does not undermine its authority, based on the Ansar's acceptance of it as a khabar al-ahad.

Core Idea
The Ansar accepted a single report; a mutawatir report would be even stronger.

This lesson focuses on a key observation in the debate over the imamate: the Quraysh hadith was not transmitted en masse (mutawatir), yet the Ansar, who initially claimed partnership, submitted to it as a single report (khabar al-ahad). This demonstrates that its non-mutawatir status is not a flaw but actually underscores its acceptance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation slowly. Notice the author's tone of wonder ('remarkable').
2
Understand the argument
The author argues that if the hadith were mutawatir, the Ansar would never have disputed. Since they did initially claim partnership, it shows the report was not mutawatir. Yet they accepted it as khabar al-ahad.
This reasoning defends the validity of the Quraysh specification despite its transmission level.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this matters: the opponent's objection loses force because the acceptance of the report by the Ansar proves its reliability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن العجب أن خبر التخصيص بقريش لم يكن متواتراً، إذ لو تواتر لما ادعت الأنصار شركة في الأمر، وهم قد انقادوا لخبر الآحاد، فكيف يظن بهم أنهم لا ينقادون للخبر المتواتر.
فإن قيل : عمر رضي الله عنه كان يجوز الإمامة لغير قريش، بل للموالي حين قال : لو كان سالم مولى حديفة حيا لما تخالجني فيه شك، وأيضا فإنكم ادعيتم أن لا نص في الإمامة، وقد ثبت نص في تخصيص قريش بأن قال: «الأئمة من قريش فما جوابكم عمن يقول : إذا ثبت نص في تخصيص قريش عن كل المسلمين فيجوز أن يثبت نص في تخصيص بني هاشم من قريش، وأيضا فإنكم ادعيتم في الأول إحالة ثبوت النص وربطتم الحكم بالإجماع ، ثم قلتم : إن الإجماع يتضمن نصا حتى يكون دليلا في الشريعة فقد ربطتم الإمامة بالنص، فهلا ادعيتم النص على أبي بكر، ولم عقدتم بابا في إبطال النص وإثبات الاختيار ؟
قيل : أما الأول فلان عمر سلم لأبي بكر ذلك الخبر الذي رواه، ولم يظهر منه أنه كان يجوز الإمامة لغير قريش وأما سالم فقد قيل: إنه كان ينسب إلى قريش، فلذلك قال : ما تخالجني فيه شك، لما شهد النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم بحسن سيرته وأمانته.
وأما النص الخفي الذي يتضمنه الإجماع فهو لعمري لازم جدا، فإن الإمامة إذا لم تثبت إلا بالإجماع، والإجماع لا يثبت إلا بالنص، فلم يثبت الإمامة إلا بالنص.
And it is remarkable that the report of specification for Quraysh was not mutawatir, for if it were mutawatir, the Ansar would not have claimed partnership in the matter, and they submitted to a khabar al-ahad (single report). So how could it be thought that they would not submit to a mutawatir report? If it is said: 'Umar, may Allah be pleased with him, used to allow the imamate for other than Quraysh, even for the mawali, when he said: If Salim, the freed slave of Hudhayfa, were alive, I would not have any doubt about him. Moreover, you claim that there is no text for the imamate, yet a text has been established specifying Quraysh by his saying: 'The imams are from Quraysh.' So what is your answer to one who says: If a text specifying Quraysh from all the Muslims is established, then it is possible that a text specifying Banu Hashim from Quraysh is established. Also, you first claimed the impossibility of the text and tied the ruling to consensus, then you said that consensus entails a text so that it would be a proof in the Sharia. Thus you have tied the imamate to a text. Why then did you not claim a text for Abu Bakr, and why did you open a chapter on invalidating the text and establishing choice?' It is said: 'As for the first, because Umar conceded to Abu Bakr that report which he narrated, and it did not appear from him that he allowed the imamate for other than Quraysh. As for Salim, it was said that he was attributed to Quraysh, so he said: 'No doubt about him,' because the Prophet, peace be upon him, testified to his good conduct and trustworthiness. As for the hidden text that consensus entails, by my life, it is very necessary. For if the imamate is not established except by consensus, and consensus is not established except by a text, then the imamate is not established except by a text.'
Source pages p. 491

Commentary
This passage continues the debate over the imamate, specifically addressing an objection based on Umar's reported statement about Salim. The author first remarks on the Quraysh hadith: it is not mutawatir (massively transmitted), but the Ansar accepted it as a khabar al-ahad (single report). This shows that if it were mutawatir, there would have been no dispute; hence its non-mutawatir status is not a problem. Then a new objection is raised: Umar allegedly allowed an imam from non-Quraysh, even a mawla such as Salim. The objection also points out inconsistency: the Sunni argument claims no explicit text for the imamate, yet they rely on the Quraysh hadith, which is a text. Furthermore, if a text for Quraysh is accepted, why not a text for Banu Hashim? And if consensus entails a text, why not claim a text for Abu Bakr? The answer clarifies that Umar's statement did not mean he opposed the Quraysh requirement; rather, he acknowledged the report about Quraysh from Abu Bakr, and Salim was considered a relative of Quraysh (by client status or lineage). Moreover, the 'hidden text' (النص الخفي) that consensus implies is necessary: the imamate is established only by consensus, and consensus itself requires a textual basis, so ultimately the imamate is grounded in a text, albeit an implicit one.

Key Terms
التواتر
خبر الآحاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Quraysh hadith must be mutawatir to be authoritative.
Correction: The author shows that even as a khabar al-ahad, the Ansar accepted it, so it does not need to be mutawatir.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why did the Ansar not initially accept the Quraysh hadith as decisive?
A) Because it was not mutawatir
B) Because they had their own candidate
C) Because they rejected single reports
D) Because they claimed partnership

Key Takeaway
The Quraysh hadith's non-mutawatir status does not weaken its authority; the Ansar's acceptance of it as a single report proves its strength.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a truth that goes against your own interests, do you still submit to it as the Ansar did, or do you look for reasons to reject it? What would you have done if you were an Ansari hearing that leadership is restricted to Quraysh?
?
Do you ever dismiss a proof as 'not strong enough' simply because it doesn't meet your preferred standard, even when sincere people before you accepted it? How can you guard against such pride in your own judgment?
?
The Ansar's acceptance of a single report shows their trust in the Prophet's ﷺ guidance. Is there an area of your life where you resist a clear command because you feel it disadvantages you? Will you today submit to it completely?

The Objection from Umar and Its Answer
Objection from Umar's statement and the author's answer regarding Salim's Quraysh connection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends the Quraysh requirement against the objection based on Umar's statement about Salim.

Core Idea
Umar's statement about Salim does not contradict the Quraysh requirement.

In this lesson, we examine a significant objection: Umar reportedly said he would have accepted Salim, a mawla, as imam. The opponent uses this to argue against the Quraysh specification. The author's response clarifies that Umar conceded to Abu Bakr's report and that Salim was considered related to Quraysh.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the opponent's argument carefully. It includes multiple points: Umar's statement, the Quraysh hadith as a text, and the potential for a text for Banu Hashim.
2
Analyze the first answer
The author responds to each point. Focus on the first answer: Umar accepted Abu Bakr's narration, so he did not reject the Quraysh requirement.
3
Understand the Salim case
The author explains that Salim was attributed to Quraysh (by mawla status or lineage), so his case does not break the rule.
This is a key clarification.
4
Reflect on the overall defense
Consider how the author defends the Sunni position without denying Umar's statement.

?

?

?

The Hidden Text and the Necessity of Text for Consensus
Conclusion on hidden text: consensus requires a text, so imamate requires a text.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the concept of 'hidden text' (النص الخفي) and why the imamate ultimately requires a textual basis according to the author.

Core Idea
Consensus implies a hidden text; imamate is rooted in divine authority.

This lesson concludes the discussion by addressing the final objection about inconsistency. The author argues that the imamate is established only through consensus, but consensus itself requires a textual basis. Therefore, there is a 'hidden text' that underlies the validity of the imamate, which resolves the apparent contradiction of rejecting explicit texts while relying on consensus.

Guided Path
1
Read the final passage
Read the Arabic and translation of the concluding argument about hidden text.
2
Understand the logical chain
The author states: imamate is established only by consensus; consensus is established only by a text; therefore imamate is established only by a text. This is a syllogism.
The 'hidden text' is the textual basis implied by consensus.
3
Apply to the objection
This answers the objection that Sunnis reject texts but accept consensus. The text is there, but hidden within consensus.
This is a sophisticated defense of the Sunni position.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this matters for the doctrine of imamate: it grounds the ruler's authority in divine guidance, not mere human choice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?

What would it look like to submit every source of authority in your life to the text?

Objections to the Quraysh Hadith and Imamate Text
Objections to the Quraysh hadith and the Sunni concept of implicit text.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key objections raised against the Sunni position that imamate is based on an implicit text in consensus, and why these objections challenge the consistency of the doctrine.

Core Idea
The Quraysh hadith is not mass-transmitted, and claiming an implicit text leads to contradictions.

This passage opens with a surprising observation: the report that imams must be from Quraysh is not mutawatir, yet the Ansar accepted a solitary report in other matters. The objector then raises several challenges: Umar's statement about Salim suggests imamate might be open to non-Quraysh, and if a text specifies Quraysh, why not also Banu Hashim? Moreover, if consensus implies a text, why not claim a direct text for Abu Bakr? These objections probe the coherence of the Sunni argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the surprising premise
Start by reading the opening line about the Quraysh hadith not being mutawatir. Notice how the author uses the Ansar's behavior as evidence.
This sets up the dialectic.
2
Identify each objection
List the three objections: (1) Umar's view on Salim, (2) extension to Banu Hashim, (3) the paradox of implicit text vs. no direct text for Abu Bakr.
The objections are embedded in 'فإن قيل' section.
3
Reflect on the strategic weight
Consider why each objection is powerful: they try to force the Sunni position into inconsistency regarding textual evidence.
The objections target both the empirics and the logic.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن العجب أن خبر التخصيص بقريش لم يكن متواتراً، إذ لو تواتر لما ادعت الأنصار شركة في الأمر، وهم قد انقادوا لخبر الآحاد، فكيف يظن بهم أنهم لا ينقادون للخبر المتواتر.
فإن قيل: عمر رضي الله عنه كان يجوز الإمامة لغير قريش، بل للموالي حين قال: لو كان سالم مولى حديفة حيا لما تخالجني فيه شك، وأيضا فإنكم ادعيتم أن لا نص في الإمامة، وقد ثبت نص في تخصيص قريش بأن قال: «الأئمة من قريش» فما جوابكم عمن يقول: إذا ثبت نص في تخصيص قريش عن كل المسلمين فيجوز أن يثبت نص في تخصيص بني هاشم من قريش، وأيضا فإنكم ادعيتم في الأول إحالة ثبوت النص وربطتم الحكم بالإجماع، ثم قلتم: إن الإجماع يتضمن نصا حتى يكون دليلا في الشريعة فقد ربطتم الإمامة بالنص، فهلا ادعيتم النص على أبي بكر، ولم عقدتم بابا في إبطال النص وإثبات الاختيار؟
قيل: أما الأول فلأن عمر سلم لأبي بكر ذلك الخبر الذي رواه، ولم يظهر منه أنه كان يجوز الإمامة لغير قريش. وأما سالم فقد قيل: إنه كان ينسب إلى قريش، فلذلك قال: ما تخالجني فيه شك، لما شهد النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم بحسن سيرته وأمانته.
وأما النص الخفي الذي يتضمنه الإجماع فهو لعمري لازم جدا، فإن الإمامة إذا لم تثبت إلا بالإجماع، والإجماع لا يثبت إلا بالنص، فلم يثبت الإمامة إلا بالنص.
والجواب أن النص الضمني في الإجماع ربما يكون نصا في الإمامة، وربما يكون نصا في أن الإجماع حجة، فتردد الأمرين بين هذين المحتملين، فلم يمكنا دعوى النص على أبي بكر، وذلك النص ربما لا يكون نصا ظاهرا لكن قرينة الحال عند القوم وهم شهود الحضرة ربما أورثتهم قطعا، فتنزل غير الظاهر عندهم كالظاهر، وحصل لهم القطع بذلك
And it is surprising that the report of specification to Quraysh was not mutawatir (mass-transmitted), for if it were mutawatir, the Ansar would not have claimed partnership in the matter, while they submitted to a solitary report (khabar al-ahad), so how could it be thought of them that they would not submit to the mutawatir report? If it is said: Umar—may Allah be pleased with him—permitted the imamate to other than Quraysh, even to the mawali (clients), when he said: 'If Salim, the mawla of Hudhayfa, were alive, I would have no doubt about him,' and also you claimed that there is no definitive text (nass) regarding the imamate, yet a text has been established specifying Quraysh by his saying: 'The imams are from Quraysh.' So what is your answer to one who says: If a text specifying Quraysh is established from all Muslims, then it is permissible that a text specifying the Banu Hashim from Quraysh is established? Also, you initially claimed the impossibility of a text being established and tied the ruling to consensus (ijma'), then you said: Consensus includes a text so that it becomes a legal proof, so you have tied the imamate to the text. Why then did you not claim the text for Abu Bakr, and why did you hold a chapter on nullifying the text and establishing election? It is said: As for the first part, Umar submitted to Abu Bakr that report which he narrated, and it did not appear from him that he permitted the imamate to other than Quraysh. As for Salim, it is said that he was attributed to Quraysh, therefore he said: 'I have no doubt about him,' because the Prophet—peace be upon him—testified to the goodness of his conduct and his trustworthiness. As for the hidden text which consensus contains, by my life, it is very necessary, for if the imamate is established only by consensus, and consensus is established only by a text, then the imamate is established only by a text. The answer is that the implicit text (al-nass al-dimni) in consensus could be a text regarding the imamate, or it could be a text that consensus is a proof. So the matter oscillates between these two possibilities, so it is not possible for us to claim the text for Abu Bakr. That text may not be an apparent text, but the circumstantial evidence (qarinat al-hal) among the people, as they were witnesses of the presence, perhaps gave them certainty, so the non-apparent becomes for them like the apparent, and they attained certainty thereby.
Source pages p. 491

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer sequence defending the Sunni position that imamate is established by consensus based on an implicit text. The passage begins by noting a surprising fact: the report that imamate is restricted to Quraysh is not mutawatir (mass-transmitted), because if it were, the Ansar would not have claimed a share in leadership. Yet the Ansar submitted to a solitary report (khabar al-ahad) in other matters, so it is unlikely they would reject a mutawatir report. The objector then raises several points: (1) Umar's statement about Salim (a non-Qurayshi) suggests he considered imamate possible for non-Quraysh. (2) If a text specifying Quraysh exists, why not also a text specifying Banu Hashim? (3) The Sunnis claim consensus implies a text, so why not directly claim a text for Abu Bakr? The answer addresses these sequentially: (1) Umar accepted Abu Bakr's report and never acted contrary to the Quraysh condition; his statement about Salim was not about imamate but about Salim's trustworthiness, and Salim was considered related to Quraysh. (2) The hidden text in consensus is necessary, because imamate is only proven by consensus, and consensus itself is only proven by a text. However, the implicit text (al-nass al-dimni) is ambiguous: it could be a text for imamate or a text establishing consensus as a proof. This ambiguity prevented a direct claim of text for Abu Bakr. (3) The circumstantial evidence (qarinat al-hal) from the Companions' presence with the Prophet gave them certainty that the text pointed to Abu Bakr, even if the text was not explicit. Thus, the non-apparent became for them like the apparent, and they attained certainty.

Key Terms
خبر الآحاد
متواتر
نص خفي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the author find surprising about the Quraysh hadith?
A) That it is weak and unreliable.
B) That it is not mutawatir, despite the Ansar accepting solitary reports.
C) That the Ansar rejected it.
D) That it was never narrated by the Companions.

Key Takeaway
The objections challenge the Sunni position by highlighting apparent contradictions in the textual basis for imamate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The objector challenges you to follow the implications of your own beliefs: if you accept the Quraysh hadith, why not also accept a text for Banu Hashim? Are you willing to trace your beliefs about leadership to their logical end, even if it leads you to a position you currently reject? What would that mean for your allegiance today?
?
Umar's willingness to consider Salim, a non-Qurayshi, shows flexibility in applying textual evidence. Is there any belief you hold about Islam that you have never re-examined because it is uncomfortable to do so? Will you commit to honestly questioning even your most cherished assumptions this week?
?
The passage exposes a contradiction: denying a definitive text while claiming consensus implies one. Do you hold any contradictory beliefs in your own faith—perhaps between what you say you believe and how you actually live? Can you name one today and seek to resolve it, even if it costs you something?

Answer: Implicit Text and Circumstantial Evidence
Author's defense of the Sunni position through implicit text and circumstantial evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends the Sunni position by clarifying Umar's stance, explaining the necessity of implicit text, and introducing circumstantial evidence as a source of certainty.

Core Idea
Consensus must be based on an implicit text, but its ambiguity prevents a direct claim for Abu Bakr; circumstantial evidence gave the Companions certainty.

In this passage, the author systematically answers each objection. He explains that Umar did not actually permit imamate for non-Quraysh and that Salim was considered related to Quraysh. More importantly, he introduces the concept of an implicit text within consensus, which is necessary but ambiguous. He concludes that circumstantial evidence from the Companions' presence with the Prophet resolved this ambiguity, allowing them to recognize Abu Bakr's imamate with certainty.

Guided Path
1
Follow the sequential answers
Read the author's responses in order: first about Umar, then about Salim, then about the hidden text, and finally about circumstantial evidence.
Each answer directly addresses the corresponding objection.
2
Understand the implicit text
The author says consensus contains a hidden text (al-nass al-dimni), but it could refer either to the imamate or to the authority of consensus itself. This ambiguity prevented a direct claim for Abu Bakr.
This is the core of the defense.
3
Grasp the role of circumstantial evidence
The Companions, through their direct experience with the Prophet, had circumstantial evidence (qarinat al-hal) that made the non-apparent like apparent, giving them certainty about Abu Bakr's imamate.
This explains how the implicit text was sufficient for them.
